FULL VIDEO: President Trump Takes Questions From Reporters On Sunday Night AF1 Flight To JBA

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·20:47 ·16.2K views · 2026-03-16
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump declared that Iran’s military is "essentially defeated" following the destruction of its air defense, navy, and manufacturing capabilities, while demanding that international beneficiaries—specifically China—take over the policing of the Strait of Hormuz.
TL;DR President Trump declared that Iran’s military is "essentially defeated" following the destruction of its air defense, navy, and manufacturing capabilities, while demanding that international beneficiaries—specifically China—take over the policing of the Strait of Hormuz. ...

TL;DR

President Trump declared that Iran’s military is "essentially defeated" following the destruction of its air defense, navy, and manufacturing capabilities, while demanding that international beneficiaries—specifically China—take over the policing of the Strait of Hormuz.

Military Degradation of Iran

  • Combat Status: President Trump asserted that the Iranian military is "decimated," citing the total neutralization of their Air Force, Navy, and air defense systems.
      • Strategic Strikes: U.S. forces attacked Kharg Island, leaving only specific oil pipe infrastructure standing as a tactical leverage point that can be destroyed "on five minutes' notice."
      • Capability Losses: Missile stockpiles are at a record low, and drone manufacturing has been reduced to approximately 20% of its original capacity.
  • Leadership Attrition: Trump noted that a significant portion of Iran's leadership has been "taken out," making diplomatic negotiations difficult as it is unclear "who you're dealing with."
  • Nuclear Deterrence: The President claimed that previous B-2 bomber strikes "obliterated" Iranian nuclear sites, preventing what he described as imminent nuclear warfare in the Middle East.

International Policing of the Strait of Hormuz

  • Burden Sharing: Trump is demanding that countries reliant on the Strait of Hormuz provide their own security and maritime policing.
      • The "China" Factor: China receives approximately 90% of its oil through the Strait, while the U.S. receives only 1–2%. Trump questioned why the U.S. continues to provide free security for China’s energy supply.
      • UK Participation: Trump criticized the UK Prime Minister's initial reluctance to deploy aircraft carriers, stating he told the UK, "I don't want them after we win the war, I want them before it started."
  • Resource Deployment: While the Iranian military is "defeated," Trump warned that "a couple terrorists" could still disrupt the Strait, necessitating international minesweepers and patrol boats.

AI Disinformation and Media Critique

  • AI-Generated Propaganda: Trump claimed Iran is using sophisticated AI to generate "fake news," including fabricated footage of an attack on the USS Abraham Lincoln and a "totally AI-generated" rally of 250,000 people supporting Khomeini.
  • Media Hostility: The President leveled heavy criticism at domestic media outlets:
      • Wall Street Journal: Accused of "false" reporting regarding the destruction of five U.S. fuel tankers; Trump clarified only one sustained slight damage.
      • ABC News: Labeled by Trump as "the most corrupt news organization on the planet."
  • Public Perception: Trump alleged that U.S. media companies are knowingly reporting AI-generated Iranian propaganda, which he described as "criminal" and "dangerous for the country."

Diplomatic Outlook and Energy Markets

  • Iran Negotiations: While Iran reportedly "wants to negotiate badly," Trump stated he is not yet ready to make a deal and has not officially declared victory.
      • Red Line: Any future deal must begin with the absolute prohibition of nuclear weapons.
  • Oil Prices: Trump predicted that energy prices will "come tumbling down" once the conflict concludes, which he expects to happen "pretty quickly."
  • Regional Relations: The President described his relationship with Israeli Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu as "extraordinary" and noted that the two militaries are "well coordinated."
  • Cuba and Venezuela: Trump signaled that a deal or significant action regarding Cuba is imminent, noting that the "failed nation" is next on the agenda after the Iran situation is resolved.

Mark Cuban on the Broken Drug Pricing System & How PBMs Really Work | The Real Eisman Playbook Ep 50

Steve Eisman(163.0K) ·51:06 ·14.0K views · 2026-03-16
Mark Cuban (Founder, Cost Plus Drugs) Steve Eisman (Host, The Real Eisman Playbook)
> "We are the only pharmacy that publishes a price list.
TL;DR: Mark Cuban is disrupting the $4 trillion U.S. healthcare industry by bypassing the opaque Pharmacy Benefit Manager (PBM) system to provide transparent, "Cost Plus 15%" pricing. He argues that the current system's vertical integration and rebate structures prioritize...

TL;DR: Mark Cuban is disrupting the $4 trillion U.S. healthcare industry by bypassing the opaque Pharmacy Benefit Manager (PBM) system to provide transparent, "Cost Plus 15%" pricing. He argues that the current system's vertical integration and rebate structures prioritize corporate earnings over patient health, effectively turning hospitals into subprime lenders.

The Structural Rot: PBMs and "Subprime" Healthcare

  • The PBM Oligopoly: Three major Pharmacy Benefit Managers (PBMs) control over 80% of the market. These entities, often owned by insurance companies, determine which drugs are "covered" (on the formulary) and at what price.
  • The Rebate Trap: PBMs demand "rebates" (essentially kickbacks) from manufacturers to grant them access to "covered lives."
      • Patient Impact: While a drug might retail for $1,000, the PBM may receive a $400 rebate. However, patients in their deductible phase must pay the full $1,000, effectively subsidizing the PBM’s profit.
  • Hospitals as Subprime Lenders: High deductibles have forced hospitals into a "subprime" role.
      • Uncollectible Debt: Hospitals "loan" patients money for care they know the patients likely cannot afford (the deductible) simply to unlock the remaining payments from the insurance company.
  • Vertical Integration Issues: Insurance giants own the doctors, the pharmacies, and the PBMs.
      • Accounting Manipulation: Cuban highlights "left-pocket/right-pocket" transfers—totaling $160 billion annually for one major insurer—which allows companies to manipulate Medical Loss Ratios by overcharging their own subsidiaries.

The Disruption: Mark Cuban Cost Plus Drugs

  • Radical Transparency: Cost Plus Drugs is the only pharmacy that publishes its actual costs. Their revenue model is fixed: Manufacturing/Acquisition Cost + 15% Markup + $5 Pharmacy Fee + $5 Shipping.
  • Comparative Savings: Cuban cites extreme examples of price gouging.
      • Example: A biosimilar for Stelara (used for Crohn's/colitis) that typically costs thousands per quarter is sold for $365 through Cost Plus.
  • Scale and Scope: The company launched in early 2022 and now serves millions of patients with approximately 2,300 drugs (5,000 SKUs). While primarily focused on generics, they are expanding into brand names (e.g., Pfizer, Bausch + Lomb) and biosimilars.
  • Market Leverage: Employers and organizations (like CalPERS) are now using the Cost Plus price list as a benchmark to force PBMs to lower their rates or match the transparent pricing.

Beyond Pharmacy: Cost Plus Wellness and Manufacturing

  • Cost Plus Wellness: A new initiative for direct contracting between employers and hospitals.
      • Eliminating Friction: By paying hospitals 5% more than insurance rates but removing "prior authorizations," denials, and audits, the system reduces administrative overhead for providers and out-of-pocket costs for employees.
      • Open Source Contracts: Cuban publishes these negotiated contracts online for free, allowing other employers to bypass insurance intermediaries.
  • Modular Manufacturing: Cuban’s partner, Alex Oshmyansky, has developed "modular pods" to manufacture sterile injectables and "N-of-1" therapies for rare diseases.
      • Cost Reduction: For rare diseases, they aim to cut manufacturing costs from $500,000 to $50,000 and reduce timelines from six months to three.

Market Outlook and Leadership Lessons

  • Investment Philosophy: Cuban believes market "fundamentals are dead," replaced by supply-and-demand momentum and "meme" behavior in everything from stocks to gold.
      • Cash Position: He is currently heavy in cash, citing high geopolitical uncertainty and waiting for a "tide goes out" moment to find value, similar to his strategy during the 2008 crisis and COVID-19.
  • Entrepreneurial Advice: Cuban applies lessons from the Dallas Mavericks and *Shark Tank* to healthcare.
      • Define the Business: He realized the Mavericks weren't in the "basketball business" but the "experience business." Similarly, Cost Plus is in the "transparency and trust business."
      • Operational Simplicity: "Sales cures all." If a product isn't easy for the customer to buy and provide immediate value, it won't survive the industry's incumbents.

Notable Quotes

"We are the only pharmacy that publishes a price list. Think about that for a second... in one of the biggest industries in the United States, one company is the only one that puts out its price list." — Mark Cuban
"Your sickest employees are the ones that pay for the rebates. By definition, if nobody used any medications, there would be zero rebates. That's wrong in every way." — Mark Cuban
"The US is the only country in the world where pharmaceutical benefit managers (PBMs) exist... It's a hidden scheme where pharma companies bribe PBMs to get on those formularies via the rebates." — Steve Eisman
"I’m more in the Warren Buffett category: when the shit hits the fan and the current goes out, you get to see who’s naked. I want to be the one with cash when that happens." — Mark Cuban

Is that the problem, or is the problem even broader than that? It's much broader than that. The problem is, and this goes back to what you're talking about, the hospitals being subprime lenders. We all are in a set of circumstances where we have to make decisions pretty much every year about our insurance. If we get it through an employer, they'll say, "Here's all the different plans," and you'll pick depending on your circumstances. Do you have a family? Are you healthy? Do you have an illness that you have to be concerned about? Whatever it may be, and you might pick a higher premium and lower deductible if you make more money, or vice versa. If you don't have it through an employer, you may get it through the ACA, you may be on Medicaid, or you may be on Medicare. But let's put aside Medicaid and Medicare because those are government services. If you're having to buy it for yourself or get it through an employer, your deductible ends up being more important than your premium. Because if you have a deductible, say it's $1,500—which is actually a low deductible—but you can't afford $1,500, it's like if a dishwasher breaks. If you can't afford to fix it, no matter what it costs, you don't have a dishwasher. If you can't afford your deductible, you don't really have insurance. But what happens when somebody who gets really ill goes to the hospital and can't afford their deductible? Typically that hospital will say, "Look, we'll loan you the money," or "Here's the third party that will loan you the money." They know that if you can't afford a $1,500 or $2,500 or $5,000 deductible, there's a very good chance you can't pay them back. They hope you can, but they know you can't, and that basically makes them a subprime lender where they're lending to people where the likelihood of them getting paid back isn't very high. But they do it because they want to help, but more importantly, they want to get access to the insurance company money. Because even if it's a $2,500 deductible, if you need your hip replaced or work on your back, they know that once you pay your deductible, there's a good chance your insurance company's going to pay the rest. They want to get to that money; that's why they'll loan it to you. That's the foundation of the system, and that's where the problems start because so many people have deductibles they can't afford. We did a survey and we asked, "What was, for if you're not on Medicaid or Medicare, what's more important to you, the premium or the deductible?" and like 60 plus percent said the deductible. Yeah, and that makes perfect sense. But things get worse from there. The way the system makes things worse, the insurance companies aren't just insurance companies. They own all these other companies including the PBMs. Just a pause, because not everybody even knows what a PBM is. PBM is the pharmaceutical benefit manager. Why don't you tell people what that entity is, because there's no other country on planet Earth that has a PBM that uses those. I know, it's crazy. So, what is a PBM and what does it do? So, what a PBM does, a pharmacy benefit manager, does just what their name implies. They manage the benefits for your insurance plan. Your insurance plan will pick any one of multiple PBMs. There's three of them that dominate and have 80 plus percent of the market. And that PBM will be responsible for setting the price that you pay during your deductible phase and outside your deductible. When you get a prescription, the pharmacist puts the prescription into their software and it goes to the PBM or one of their companies who determines if the insurance company they work with will pay for that claim. So, that's effectively what they do. They'll also negotiate prices with brand medications so that they'll determine how much Eloquis will be for the patient and how much the employer will pay if the employer's paying for it. Those are the basic things that they do. They manage all that. But, and that sounds legit, right? It totally sounds legit. Yeah, it sounds legit. The problem is they put their earnings per share way above the patient. Now, they'll disagree obviously, but they charge rebates and they charge fees to the brand manufacturers. They may pay the manufacturer for a drug $100 and then they'll charge the employer $200, which in turn makes your premium more expensive for your boss to pay for your insurance, right? They'll negotiate contracts with—well, or the insurance company that they're part of will negotiate contracts with the hospital, going beyond drugs. Let's just say the price they negotiate for a hip replacement is $25,000 with a local hospital. And you need a hip replacement and you just borrowed $5,000 to hit your deductible and now you owe another $20,000 for your hip replacement that the insurance company's supposed to pay. The insurance company won't pay the full $25,000. The insurance company will underpay them, they'll short pay them, then they'll late pay them, and then they'll do all these other things in audits and everything so that it becomes more expensive for the hospital to do business with your insurance company. Which in turn makes the cost of care more expensive for everybody because now the hospitals invent bullshit that they do too. They create these things called, you know, facilities fees where they'll charge you just for using the hospital. Just for walking in. Just for, you know, well, yeah, for getting any type of care done, they'll jack it up. They'll do these things where if the care is offered on the fourth floor of the building and they happen to own that doctor or that doctor works for them, they'll charge you more than if that doctor didn't work for them and was in a different office, right? They go to the manufacturers of the big name drugs—the GLP-1s, Eloquis, Xarelto, etc. And they say, "Hey, if you want to have access to all those 60 million lives that we cover, you have to write us a check and it's going to be called a rebate." You're going to pay us so that you can have access to all those members. If you don't... You're not on the formulary. Yeah, you're not on the formulary and we're not going to pay for it or we're going to pay a lot less than you want to be paid. So, the drug might wholesale for $1,000 and the rebate might be for $400. So, here's the question: who is paying? Is the consumer paying $1,000 for the drug? Am I paying $600 for the drug, or am I paying somewhere in between? So first of all, in pharmacy, the wholesale price is the retail price. It's a mess. Wholesale, yeah, and they call it WAC: wholesale acquisition cost equals retail. For a $1,000 retail drug, if you are in your deductible phase—so it's January, you have an ACA plan with a $5,000 deductible. You have ACA silver, let's say. That drug is $1,000. In January when you need your prescription, you're paying $1,000. Now you have $4,000 left on your deductible. In February, you're paying $1,000. You have $3,000, etc., etc., until you've paid off your deductible. Then they have what's called a copay. But regardless, of that $1,000 retail price, there's the whole rebate amount that was negotiated, and that goes to the PBM. Not to the consumer? Because he said it again and again and again that that $400 goes to the consumer. He basically said that the consumer pays $600. No. Because what happens is they get control of it and then they have these things called group purchasing organizations that they own that negotiated that rebate. That group purchasing organization will keep a vig, if you will—a fee for doing that negotiation even though they own the company—and that fee will be 10 to 20% of the thousand. So, they'll keep $200. And so, now you've got $200 left in rebate and typically they'll pass that on to the employer who then either keeps it or uses it to buy down the cost of premiums. But here's the messed up part, Steve. Either way, however that rebate is used, that means the patient—particularly during their deductible phase—your sick patient is paying for the rebate. They're paying the $1,000. And if $400 of that is rebate, they're paying that rebate and then the PBM is getting it and keeping some and sending some to the employer. That's wrong in every way and that's why I went nuts on him. Most employers don't even understand this is how it works. When I talk to CEOs and benefit people, they were like, "Oh no, no, no, that's not right." Your sickest employees are the ones that pay for the rebates. By definition, because if nobody used any medications, there would be zero rebates. And that's just wrong. When I tell CEOs that and they realize it, they're now starting to understand that this is a problem. And then I start to scare the shit out of them because there's been some tragic things that have happened because of the way the system is designed. I was at a conference and one of the insurance brokers told a story about how there was a transportation company that had good insurance but there was a truck driver who couldn't afford their medication during the deductible phase. I want that, because that's when I've always made my most money by being patient. I used to trade stocks all the time, and I did really well. But now there's so much money and so much advanced technology that it's hard to differentiate. I think the bias is to go up, but I'm concerned right now just with geopolitics of everything. There's 100% uncertainty now because nobody can predict everything that's going to happen and that scares me. I've actually pulled back some; maybe I'll miss some of the upswing. I don't care, because if it does hit the fan like in the great financial crisis—I saw the subprime stuff happening, I went mostly in cash and I killed it. When COVID hit I went all in when all the stocks dropped. I can be patient. I think it was Charlie Munger who said the best approach is delayed gratification. If you can delay your gratification and not get the gamble bug when it comes to trading stocks—and that's hard because it's fun to trade—but if you can delay that gratification, when something unfortunate does happen, if you're the one with cash, you're the one that makes the money. So just to finish up, let's go back to your company and the drug world. What message would you like people to get about what you're doing? Thank you for asking, Steve. For any medication that you're taking—with insurance, without insurance, or for your family—go to costplusdrugs.com. Look it up to see if we carry it and compare the price to your copay, your coinsurance, or your deductible to see if we can save you money. And if you are an employer or a CEO or entrepreneur, do the same thing. Go to your PBM and say, "Look, we'd like to add Cost Plus Drugs to our network." Worst case, you're going to save money. Mark, thank you very much for your time. Hopefully maybe in some future date we could do this again. I hope so; it was a lot of fun, Steve. I really appreciate it. Thank you. That was a really interesting interview with Mark Cuban, and we drilled down into the lack of transparency in the healthcare industry, runaway pricing in the pharmaceutical industry, and deductibles, which are totally unaffordable. Drug prices in the United States are just way too high, and many people cannot afford the medicine they need to stay healthy and to stay alive. Mark's solution is to create an alternative drug delivery system with embedded transparency in pricing. He sets pricing at 15% above wholesale. It's a clean solution for an extremely convoluted industry, and he is reaching through the morass of special interests and hidden deal-making to help people. To really understand what is wrong with the pharmaceutical industry, we have to understand the players and how they interact. Here are my major takeaways from the interview. The US is the only country in the world where pharmaceutical benefit managers, PBMs, exist. Most people have no idea what they are and the huge role they play in making drug prices much higher in the US than everywhere else. It's a hidden scheme where pharma companies sell their drugs to wholesalers at what is called the wholesale price. That price is actually the retail price, the price that consumers pay. PBMs then negotiate rebates from the pharma companies. But those rebates usually do not go to the consumer. They get pocketed largely by the PBMs, and those rebates are one reason why drug prices are so high in the United States. PBMs also negotiate with pharma companies about whether their drug will be on the PBM's preferred list of drugs, called the preferred formulary. Essentially, pharma companies bribe PBMs to get on those formularies via the rebates. Being off the formulary means that a drug will just not be used. And the PBMs are largely owned by the large health insurance companies. Another major problem is that many Americans cannot, unfortunately, afford their deductibles. If a consumer has a high deductible, say $4,000, and a drug is within the deductible and the consumer cannot afford the deductible, they cannot afford the drug. That's why providers have become the new subprime lenders. Someone goes to a hospital to get care, but if they cannot afford their deductible, they cannot pay. So the hospital essentially is lending the patient the money, knowing they will never get it back. In the end, the central problem is that the interests of the health insurance industry are not aligned with consumers and patients. Mark is hoping that by only charging cost plus 15% to all consumers, and by publishing those prices, he can help drive drug costs down. I hope he succeeds. Regarding the Mavericks and what Mark learned from Shark Tank, he leaned into the lessons of first and foremost: be nice. It's a solid lesson we can all take away from this episode. Your business will be more successful if you are kind to everyone. I hope you learned a lot from this interview. I know I certainly did. See you soon.

Matt Damon and Gary White: Why the Global Water Crisis Is Really a Finance Problem

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·32:29 ·363 views · 2026-03-16
Matt Damon (Co-founder, Water.org) Gary White (Co-founder, Water.org)
> "Imagine curing cancer, and in a hundred years, a million people a year were still dying from it.
TL;DR: The global water crisis is a systemic finance failure rather than a technical one; by treating water access as a microfinance opportunity rather than a charitable burden, Water.org has empowered 85 million people through an "economic flywheel" that turns philanthropic...

TL;DR: The global water crisis is a systemic finance failure rather than a technical one; by treating water access as a microfinance opportunity rather than a charitable burden, Water.org has empowered 85 million people through an "economic flywheel" that turns philanthropic capital into sustainable investment.

The Finance-First Approach to Global Water

  • Market-Based Solutions: Gary White and Matt Damon argue that the 2.1 billion people lacking water access represent a systems failure in "financial plumbing."
      • WaterCredit: A specialized microfinance solution that provides small, affordable loans to the "economically vulnerable" to fund household water connections and toilets.
      • High Repayment Rates: Despite perceptions of risk, 98% of these loans are repaid, with 90% of borrowers being women.
  • Capital Efficiency: Traditional charitable well-drilling costs roughly $25 per person. Water.org’s model has reduced the philanthropic cost of capital to $5 per person because the money is recycled as loans are repaid.
  • WaterEquity: An asset manager founded to bridge the gap between global capital markets and local providers. It has raised approximately $500 million in committed capital across six funds to provide debt and equity to financial institutions and water entrepreneurs.

Shifting from Charity to Economic Empowerment

  • Unlocking Human Potential: Damon emphasizes that water access is a prerequisite for education and economic participation, particularly for women and girls who bear the brunt of water collection.
  • The "Mama Florence" Case Study: A 70-year-old Ugandan woman used a $300 loan to sink a well, which subsequently allowed her to:
      • Grow a vegetable garden for nutrition.
      • Raise livestock using vegetable scraps.
      • Manufacture and sell bricks using the available water and clay soil.
      • Build rental rooms for additional income.
  • Eliminating "Stupid Deaths": Access to clean water saves an estimated 1 million lives annually. Damon notes that since the technical solutions were solved a century ago, these deaths are entirely unnecessary.

Scaling Through Innovation and Partnership

  • The "Get Blue" Initiative: Launched at the 2026 Davos Annual Meeting, this consumer-facing campaign—inspired by the (RED) initiative—partners with major corporations to drive awareness and funding.
      • Initial Partners: Gap, Starbucks, Amazon, and Ecolab.
      • Consumer Connection: The campaign targets younger generations who want to "purchase with purpose," connecting a product purchase (like a blue-branded coffee or sweatshirt) directly to water access for someone in the developing world.
  • The Role of the Private Sector: Companies are encouraged to move beyond traditional philanthropy and view water as a "pre-competitive" sustainability issue that requires creative market-based interventions.

Leadership and 17 Years of Collaboration

  • Evolution of the Model: The organization transitioned from a standard NGO (Water Partners, founded 1990) to a global financial institution. Gary White notes that he would now trade two of his three engineering degrees for a finance degree to better solve the crisis.
  • The Value of Failure: The co-founders credit their success to a "New Ventures Fund" that allows for experimentation. White highlights that leading with failure—such as early struggles to loan directly to NGOs—led to the more successful strategy of de-risking existing microfinance banks.
  • Complementary Skillsets:
      • Gary White (CEO): Provides technical engineering and financial structural expertise.
      • Matt Damon (Co-founder): Leverages his platform for storytelling to bridge the "awareness gap" for a problem that feels distant to those in the developed world.

Notable Quotes

"Imagine curing cancer, and in a hundred years, a million people a year were still dying from it. It would just be what our friend Bono refers to as 'stupid death.' It's entirely unnecessary and wrong." — Matt Damon
"Nothing matters when you wake up in the morning until you get water. Nothing else matters. If you are always behind the curve because you're spending your energy just getting water, you're never going to move on and develop economically." — Gary White
"We're kind of like financial plumbers that connect capital with people who need access to water. When you can use charity as a springboard to the financial markets, that's when you can really solve big humanitarian issues." — Gary White
"If you suddenly unleash the potential of a quarter of the human species, we don't know what would happen... we don't know what we've lost already and what we'll continue to lose." — Matt Damon

That is a very special organization that has improved water access and sanitation for 85 million people. 85 million. They are here to tell us how the heck they did that, but also to break down what's really needed for scale and what they have learned in 17 years of partnership since founding this organization. 17 years. My goodness. "We can almost vote," Matt says. You can almost vote. Exactly. It is older than "We Bought a Zoo." Yes. Yeah, that's right. Exactly. Why don't we get started here? Can you guys give the world a grade? How are we doing on protecting water? Well, if you're talking about access for human beings around the world to clean water, 2.1 billion people still lack access. So one in four human beings do not have access to clean water. So, I would probably say that's an F. What about you, Gary? What grade? Yeah, I think so. I think when you put it in the context that we need to put it in, and that is where is humanity overall, it's definitely an F. I think that, you know, we do see a lot of people focusing on water as a natural resource and trying to make sure it's more equitably allocated. So, I'm hoping that we're going to trend upward ahead. Absolutely. What things are contributing to this problem? Well, I think one of the things that we see as contributing significantly to it is a lack of the financial resources going into it. You know, when you think about water and safe water, in the United States and Western Europe, we've known how to make water safe for 100 years. So, it's not necessarily that we need a silver bullet or, you know, we have to invent a new cure for this. What we need to do is ensure that we are allocating financial resources towards this, that the utilities that supply our water are well-run, and that people are billing and paying for water so that the infrastructure can be maintained. A lot of these things are systems failures, and a lot of that is rooted back in finance. That's why everything we do at water.org, or in WaterEquity, is focused on driving financial resources to solving SDG number six. Is there a statistic on this topic that would completely shock people? Something that really speaks to you that you're like, "Gosh, when we say this to people, their eyes just widen"? Well certainly the 2.1 billion people, which is one in four lacking access, that's pretty arresting. I think also a million people dying a year because of this, because of lack of access to clean water and sanitation. That's really incredibly alarming and unconscionable in 2024. You know, given that as Gary said, we solved this for ourselves a 100 years ago. Imagine curing cancer and in a hundred years, a million people a year were still dying from cancer. It would just be what our friend Bono refers to as stupid death. You know, it's just entirely unnecessary and stupid and plain wrong. You guys do a fair amount of imagining this, but a lot of us maybe haven't had the chance. What does the world look like if we get water right? You know, it's impossible to say because if you suddenly unleash the potential of a quarter of the human species, we don't know what would happen. Girls aren't in school because of this. So, if their life revolves around just surviving to the next day and not getting an education and imagining the life of possibility in front of them and going and living to the fullest, we don't know what we've lost already and what we'll continue to lose. Yeah. I mean, there's so much negative about this issue, but then to flip it around and I think it's a great question—what's the upside if people do get water? There was a woman I met in Uganda a few years ago. She was a grandmother in her 70s and just went by the name of Mama Florence. What she had been doing is getting on her bike every day and riding with jerry cans to try to scavenge for water. What we do is we help women like her get access to small, affordable loans so that they can get the solutions that are best for them. So, you know, she needed about $300 to sink a well and put a pump in there. And once she had that, she was able to then start giving her family safe water. But she also started growing a vegetable garden. So, she had vegetables to improve the health of her family. The scraps from the vegetables she was then feeding to pigs to raise pigs for her family and for income. And then, she figured out that the soil around her house was clay and that she could take the water and mix it and form bricks and start selling bricks. Ultimately, she used the bricks to build some rooms on her plot of land that she would then rent out. So here's this woman who, you know, it took to her 70s to have her kind of entrepreneurial spirit unleashed. And the reason it was is because of water. And that's the thing, you know, nothing matters when you wake up in the morning until you get water. Nothing else matters. And so if you are always kind of behind the curve because you're spending so much of your energy, time, and resources just getting water, it's never going to let you move on and develop economically. We have some sense of what that would look like, but if we could unleash the potential of those 2.1 billion people, I think the planet would look very different and look prosperous. And it's a beautiful story because of what she did. But think of it the other way. What if she had had access from the time she was seven rather than 70? What would she have done with that life, right? And so that's what we're talking about right now for young women all over the world. You guys make the point in the book that you co-wrote, "The Worth of Water," that ultimately the water crisis is a finance problem. Can you tell us a little bit about that? That's kind of our origin story. You know, we partnered and we were doing direct impact work like most people in the water space, but direct impact work is well drilling. We were very good at that, actually. Half of all water projects fail within 5 years. Ours did not because of a lot of trial and error that Gary went through. He started this in 1990. And so from what he learned and how to implement these systems, he was doing it very successfully. But when we partnered, he had kind of had this epiphany that there was never going to be enough charity in the world to get this done. If you looked at the scale of the problem and the numbers that charity could reach, you were faced with just a sad truth. As he said to me, we can continue to be like ostriches with our head in the sand or we can innovate. He had already started to do that. And he pioneered this idea that we call WaterCredit, which is essentially taking what Muhammad Yunus pioneered at the Grameen Bank with micro-loaning and redirecting it towards the water space. That was a whole journey to bring the micro-finance institutions along because they did not believe and they did not have faith that loans would be repaid for water because they're not standard income-generating loans. They're income-enhancing loans because you're buying somebody's time back. The time that they're wasting going and collecting water, the time that they're wasting going and queuing up at a water point, you know, which is terribly inefficient. Also, the insight that he had was people were actually already paying for water in these communities, right? And sometimes 25% of their income. They had no savings, but day in and day out they would pay. So his hypothesis was that if you could front a loan to these people, they would pay it back because also nobody takes out a loan for something they don't want. This would be a sustainable solution that worked locally for this person in that community. And that idea was unproven when we started. And it's been just such a success story. I mean, it's beyond anything we could have hoped. You know, 98% of these loans pay back. And that's astonishing when you realize that these are the most economically vulnerable people on the planet, mostly women, by the way. 90% of our borrowers are women, because this is an issue that disproportionately affects women and girls. And it turns out if you just nudge the market towards them and get out of their way, they will solve their own problems. And that's what we've seen. We've done over 15 million of these loans and they pay back at 98%. So that's a very real number. And that success led us into the next stage of our journey. What is the overall impact that WaterCredit has had? Well, WaterCredit has basically taken the model that Matt has talked about where we have these financial partners around the world that are making household level loans. So we have over 200 financial partners around the world that now are making loans. And that's because we've used our philanthropic capital in a way that nudges the market. It de-risks some of these portfolios and provides technical assistance to MFIs—these micro-finance banks—so that they can market the loans. What happened was that the demand for these loans really blew up in a way that a lot of these financial partners needed more capital. Matt and I were in India in 2015, quizzing all of our partners about what's holding them back, and they said we need more capital, more affordable access to capital. So that's where we kind of hatched this idea in the back of a jeep to start a fund to actually provide debt to some of those partners. That worked really well. And then we decided, let's blow this up. And so we launched an asset manager that was then named WaterEquity. WaterEquity raises debt and equity in the US and Europe in a way that we can connect it to those financial institutions. They connect it to the women who need water and toilets, and then the funds come back up the chain so that we can provide financial returns to investors, and they get tremendous social impact with that as well. And so we've now done six funds and we've brought in about a half a billion dollars in committed capital to be able to scale this up. To me, that's the evolution. That's what we do at water.org; we evolve and we innovate. Talk about getting the head out of the sand. What we see is the only way we're going to solve this crisis in the long term is if we can connect women who need water with the capital markets through WaterEquity. And that's exactly what we've done. We're kind of like financial plumbers that connect that capital with people who need access to water. Along that value chain, everybody benefits. And then when you can use charity to then use it as a springboard to the financial markets, that's when you can really solve big humanitarian issues. You guys have kind of gone from, in the very beginning, thinking about, "Hey, is it just about getting more wells?" to doing things like de-risking loan portfolios. Right. That's what we love saying because it's not where we saw ourselves ending up and it's where we did end up. But I will say that there's another statistic I love, which is had we just stayed drilling wells, which again we were kind of best-in-class there, it would have taken us 600 years just to get to where we are right now and we're accelerating. We're reaching a million people every six weeks. So many well-meaning projects, as you know, were mentioned about the wells. Good things, but very difficult to scale, right? And we've seen this in the sustainability space. In your experience, are there maybe two or three elements or ingredients that an idea or a concept needs to make sure that it can scale? Well, I think to level set a little bit, there is a need for kind of this direct impact in wells. Wells can be done in a way that are sustainable, and the problem is the financial resources for that. If we really want to scale to solve the problem, what we see is solutions like WaterCredit and financial innovation that recognizes that everyone in the world isn't too poor and that they're not equally poor and all in need of charity. So, let's carve that off and figure out how can we reach hundreds of millions of people with access to finance. That way, the government resources are then freed up to be directed to like having really good well-drilling programs and really sustainable programs. Because if you can put the resources behind that to make sure that the communities are in charge of those projects, and to make sure that they know how to maintain those projects, then I think we can have a scaled solution that meets the needs of the most destitute. Maybe those in rural areas of Ethiopia, for whom WaterCredit doesn't work, but there's enough subsidy coming in now because so many people are served through the market mechanism. That's kind of the classic applying capitalism in a way that is serving everybody from the base of the pyramid on up. So basically what you're saying is that we have to kind of build that flywheel by making sure that we're part of the market. Is that what you're saying? Exactly. You have a special initiative that just launched at Davos. Can you talk a little bit about GetBlue? Sure. Yeah. We're very excited about this. We came here to announce it. It's a partnership. Imagine going into Starbucks and instead of buying a cup of coffee with, you know, the green siren on it, you buy a cup of coffee with a blue siren, or they'll put us up on their app, which tens of millions of people engage with every single day. It's a way to try to move this on the awareness front into the public consciousness, as well as a way to raise money that will be directed to water.org or directly into these programs. In our programs, we've driven the philanthropic cost of capital to reach each person down. The standard in the water space is $25 to get somebody water for life. In our programs, it's $5 because of the nature of the program. Because it's a loan that goes out, and as it's repaid, it goes out again. So every time it goes out, the cost to reach each person goes down. For just $5, you're getting somebody on the other side of the earth clean water for life. How does this help maybe bridge this awareness gap that people have where probably a lot of people in the rich world don't really think very much about water at all? This is the first hurdle we always have to clear, is awareness, right? Because it's such an unrelatable problem for those of us who grew up in, as you call it, the rich world. You're never that far from a drink of water. And as such, you can't really relate to this problem. If you were to raise money for AIDS or cancer research, everybody's been touched personally by these things through their family or their friends. And so, we all can relate to that. It really resonates with us on an emotional level. Whereas this just feels more like an intellectual concern because, even if you hear the magnitude of the problem, it seems so overwhelming and also so distant. What we're hoping with GetBlue is that it really moves it into the public consciousness because of the ubiquity of the campaign, and it just becomes something that everybody in the developed world starts to become aware of—particularly the younger generations, who the data shows really want to purchase with purpose. And so that's the hope behind this movement. There's a lot of talk about how to really have solutions for the future, especially sustainable solutions. We need to have new types of partnerships, right? Ones that maybe are pre-competitive, ones that people wouldn't have thought about 20 years ago. When you're looking at campaigns like this or other things that you guys have put together, what's important for the private sector to be considering when it comes to being creative on how they can tackle sustainability? Well, I think the private sector is interested in solutions that work. I think the private sector recognizes—yes, they do want to be philanthropic, but they know that it doesn't end there. And so I think they're looking for solutions that kind of resonate with them and with business and with the markets. GetBlue is just a culmination of taking those relationships to a whole different level that goes beyond just their philanthropy and into their communities. We work through local talent, local experts, local institutions, and we partner with them and help them get the resources that they need to develop these loan portfolios and to have impact. There's so many entrepreneurs out there that want to solve this crisis, but they lack the capital. It's all about partnerships and trying to figure out how do you position yourself as a catalyst to put all these things together so it sparks from top to bottom. Absolutely. You talked a little bit about partnership. So water.org—now, correct me if my numbers are wrong here—water.org is more than 30 years old, right? Is that correct? Yeah, 1990. Well, Matt and I started water.org by merging our organizations in 2009. I started WaterPartners back in 1990, and Matt had started in 2006, but then joined with Gary in '09. Gary, tell us a little bit about your background so people understand. I first learned about this crisis when I was an undergrad in engineering, and you know, I was looking for this intersection between engineering and social justice. Once I discovered the water crisis, it was like, that's what I'm going to do. I started an organization at my university that would allow engineers to travel and volunteer. And as Matt has talked about our well-drilling, I thought—I got three engineering degrees, right? I would trade in at least two of those for a finance degree because this is a completely different playing field than what I thought it was going to be. But you know, that engineering background also put in me this fierce problem-solving kind of activated gene, and that's what the success of this is about. It's about finding solutions and that's what we're about. We were a water NGO. Now we're a global financial institution with an asset manager. I mean, how crazy is that? But, you know, it works because more and more people are coming in with the philanthropy, and then that's catalyzing the investment. So we'll be up to billions of dollars of assets under management in a few years to be able to tackle this problem. And Matt, you had your own water organization. Tell us briefly about the work you were doing. When I first learned about the crisis, I started with a few other people something called H2O Africa. Our plan at the time, even though we knew this isn't going to be enough, but as a stop-gap measure, it's doing something, and I know I can use my platform to raise money to funnel it to NGOs that we identify that are doing really good work across Africa. So that's what we did for a few years until I made the decision that I needed to find the pre-eminent partner in the space who was just the absolute expert. That led me very quickly to Gary, and that's when we met in 2008 and we realized how aligned we were with our mission. So we joined forces in '09 and rebranded ourselves as water.org. What did you both see in each other's capabilities and maybe interest that you felt like, "Gosh, I'll be able to scale my impact if I partner with this individual"? Well, what I saw in Matt when I first met him was his curiosity and the questions and the probing. I could tell just by the questions he was asking about our model and what we were doing, and then listening to the answers and then asking different questions. That just demonstrated to me that the intellect was there, and he had already demonstrated the passion and putting his time into it by starting another nonprofit. So it was just a matter of, how do we work together? Gary came exactly as advertised as the expert in this space, and I think there were a number of things. I did ask him every question I could think of and he had an answer for everything. He had already been doing this for a couple decades. He had a huge wealth of experience and he talked really openly about failures. He actually led with those because he explained the lesson that would come out of each one. "Well, I did this and this didn't work and here's why." And then he started to talk about market-based solutions. That's a thought leap for somebody coming into this and going, "Well, wait a minute. We're talking about the poorest people in the world. You want to give them a loan?" Very quickly, he just explained people in these communities are paying already. This loan that they pay off is going to be less than they're actually already paying for water. And within two years, once the loan is complete, they're connected to the system and they're completely free and clear of this daily expense that's burdening them right now. It was counter-intuitive until he told me that, and then it made total sense. And then there was that whole journey of proving it, right? And that took a lot of time. It wasn't until 2012 that we hit our first million people, which was a big celebration for us—a million people. But he had started in 1990. So it was 22 years in the making. And now, like we said, it's every six weeks that we hit a million people. So it's really been this wonderful, wonderful partnership. And it's one of the greatest decisions I ever made in my life was partnering with Gary because I was looking to maximize the impact that I personally could have, and nobody could have done it more. I love this idea of leading with failure when it comes to considering innovation. Why is that so important? Well, because if you're not failing sometimes you're not experimenting. You're not probing. So yeah, we could have kept with direct impact in drilling wells. And we wouldn't have failed probably very often at all, you know, because that becomes kind of like making widgets, you know, you just stamp one more out and you go on. But you can understand when you sit with the woman and talk to her about how did she get water today. That's a humbling experience. Am I really an expert? No, she's the expert because somehow every day she finds water for her family, and it's hard. Then you understand, you start to peel back the layers like, "How far did you have to walk?" and "What would you do if you didn't have to walk for..." Well, I would go to work over here because I have a friend who's doing this job and I could do that too. I met an elderly woman who was in her 80s and she had taken out a loan from a loan shark, was paying 125% interest just so she could build a toilet and have privacy of that toilet in her home. I met a woman in the Philippines who was paying $2 a day to a water vendor because she didn't have a connection to the water system in her slum. She didn't have the $300 it cost to connect, but she had $2 every day to buy water. She was paying $60 a month for that. She took out a loan and her loan payments were $5 a month. Her water bill now is $5 a month. So you basically absorb these lessons from people who are living in water poverty and then that's when you start to experiment. Now it's not a straight line. I remember the first time we made a foray into this, we were trying to make loans to water NGOs in Kenya who were very much accustomed to just getting grants. We made loans to them and they were going to work with communities and the communities weren't well-trained, and so half the loans had to be written off. We're like, okay, we're not going to be a bank. That was a dumb idea. Basically, what we're going to do is piggyback onto those financial institutions that already have banking relationships with these individuals and with these communities. But that was a hard slog because those micro-finance banks would make a loan for a cow, for instance. They would do that because you're milking the cow by the end of the week and you're selling milk, you got cash flow, and you repay the loan. These MFIs would say, "There's no way we're going to loan for a water connection because it's not income-generating." That's where we had to kind of provide this nudge and basically share risk with them to then experiment to develop new loan portfolios that would target all of these different water and sanitation solutions. And then that's what it took. It took us de-risking financial institutions instead of trying to be one. And that's when it really sparked and took off. So there was built into our whole plan a New Ventures Fund to pilot things and see what the next WaterCredit might be. It's always about exploring ways in which we can maximize impact. Well, what I've learned is you can't just be a wonky engineer and move people's hearts and minds. And of course, that's what Matt does in spades. ArsenalOS: Architecture from Design to Deployment | Northrop Grumman at AIPCon 9

TL;DR Northrop Grumman has partnered with Palantir to launch ArsenalOS, an integrated operating system that connects the entire value chain—from design and supply chain to manufacturing—enabling the delivery of advanced defense systems at "mission speed." By utilizing AI-driven...

TL;DR

Northrop Grumman has partnered with Palantir to launch ArsenalOS, an integrated operating system that connects the entire value chain—from design and supply chain to manufacturing—enabling the delivery of advanced defense systems at "mission speed." By utilizing AI-driven tools, the company has compressed development cycles, exemplified by the "Lumberjack" autonomous system moving from concept to flight in just 14 months.

ArsenalOS: A Unified Defense Architecture

  • Integrated Value Chain: ArsenalOS serves as a single digital architecture connecting every node of production, including design, supply chain, manufacturing, and deployment.
  • Strategic Objective: The platform aims to compress the cycle from "foundry to flight" without compromising the safety and exquisite engineering standards of Northrop Grumman.
  • Core Tools:
      • Supplier Risk Nucleus & PlanBuilder: Real-time tools used to connect the value chain and identify bottlenecks before they impact production.
      • Strategic Investment: Northrop Grumman has invested over $13.5 billion in R&D and infrastructure over the last five years to support this digital transformation.

Designing for Producibility

  • Producibot AI: A specialized tool that ingests engineering designs to flag complexity, supply chain risks, and lead time issues before a design is finalized.
  • Engineering Efficiency: By automating routine checks, the system has eliminated over two hours of manual work per drawing across thousands of drawings annually.
  • Mitigating Delays: Design choices that previously extended delivery paths by months are now flagged and mitigated before a purchase order is ever placed.
      • Example: Identifying prohibitive lead times for printed wiring boards early in the design phase to avoid production bottlenecks.

Proactive Supply Chain Management

  • Shift from Reactive to Proactive: Rather than asking why a delivery is late, the system identifies where delays *will* happen, allowing for early intervention.
  • Automated Remediation: When a risk is identified, ArsenalOS can suggest replacement suppliers (e.g., Dallas Defense Systems), update the Bill of Materials (BOM), and notify procurement automatically.
  • Human-in-the-loop: While the system automates data flow, humans remain responsible for high-level judgment calls, such as approving qualification waivers and budget adjustments.

"Warp Speed" Manufacturing

  • Predictive Traceability: The factory floor focuses on anticipating challenges and maximizing machine uptime rather than just fixing problems after they occur.
  • MRP Speed: Replaces the "hidden factory" of manual spreadsheets and ERP systems with AI-driven scheduling and live material movement tracking across 41 active orders and 12 manufacturing zones.
  • Cycle Time Reduction: In one showcased example, a component's complexity score was reduced from 69 to 49, allowing it to be cleared for production and manufactured within two weeks.

Case Study: The Lumberjack UAS

  • Rapid Development: Lumberjack, a low-cost one-way attack uncrewed aircraft system, was developed from concept to flight in only 14 months.
  • Embedded AI: The platform integrates Palantir’s Maven Smart system to deliver autonomous capabilities directly to the warfighter.
  • Proven Pipeline: Lumberjack serves as the primary proof of concept for the ArsenalOS pipeline, demonstrating that "exquisite" technology can be delivered with "affordable mass."

A 7-hour marathon interview with Saining Xie: World Models, AMI Labs, Yann LeCun, Fei-Fei Li, and 42

Zhang Xiaojun Podcast(17.4K) ·404:38 ·6.0K views · 2026-03-16
Saining Xie (Co-founder, AMI Labs) Zhang Xiaojun (Host, Zhang Xiaojun Podcast)
> "Silicon Valley is very LLM-pilled...
TL;DR: Saining Xie and Turing Award winner Yann LeCun have launched AMI Labs with a record-breaking $1.03B seed round to pivot from "LLM-pilled" language modeling toward "World Models"—autonomous systems capable of predictive reasoning and physical understanding rather than mere...

TL;DR: Saining Xie and Turing Award winner Yann LeCun have launched AMI Labs with a record-breaking $1.03B seed round to pivot from "LLM-pilled" language modeling toward "World Models"—autonomous systems capable of predictive reasoning and physical understanding rather than mere pattern matching.

AMI Labs: The Thesis of Advanced Machine Intelligence

  • The Funding Milestone: By March 2026, AMI Labs secured a $1.03 billion Seed round at a $3.5 billion valuation, the second-largest globally among "AI Neo Labs" (trailing only Mira Murati’s Thinking Machines).
  • A "Research First" Organization: Despite zero products and only 25 members, the lab aims to solve the "upstream" problem of building a "predictive brain." Xie argues that current industrial labs are trapped in a "finite game" of leaderboard competition, losing the ability to define new problems.
  • The World Model vs. Word Model:
      • Xie defines the current era as "LLM-pilled," where Silicon Valley mistakenly treats language as the foundation of intelligence.
      • He argues that language is a low-bandwidth communication tool (10-100 bits/sec) whereas the human visual system processes high-bandwidth signals (up to 1 billion bits/sec).
      • The goal is to move from "Word Models" (LLMs) to "World Models" that model the continuous, high-dimensional, noisy physical world.

The Critique of LLMs and "The Bitter Lesson"

  • Language as a "Crutch": Xie describes LLMs as an "opiate" or "crutch"—useful for shortcuts but preventing the development of "muscles" for physical reasoning. LLMs model the *label* space (P(y)), while true intelligence requires modeling the *data* space (P(x)).
  • The Myth of Scaling: While LLM advocates cite "The Bitter Lesson" (the power of compute over human-designed tricks), Xie contends that LLMs are actually "anti-Bitter Lesson" because they rely on highly structured, human-tokenized language data.
  • Flaws in Alignment: Current AI safety is achieved through post-training (RLHF), which is "fundamentally flawed." A true world model would be inherently safe because it can *foresee* the causal consequences of its actions and choose to avoid them.

Research Philosophy: Representation and Nonlinearity

  • Representation Learning: Xie identifies himself primarily as a "Representation Learner," a timeless theme compared to ephemeral trends like Neural Architecture Search (NAS).
  • The Nonlinear Process: Xie describes a typical 6-month research cycle where the first 5 months often involve "mental collapse" and failure, followed by a sudden burst of insight.
      • The ResNeXt Example: A month before the deadline, Xie and Kaiming He pivoted to create ResNeXt, which introduced Mixture of Experts (MoE) logic to vision.
      • The DiT Breakthrough: Diffusion Transformers (DiT) was born in the final month of a FAIR project after Xie realized U-Nets were not scalable for the next generation of generative models.
  • He Kaiming’s "Baseline" Strategy: Xie attributes his rigor to Kaiming He, who taught that "the ceiling of your research depends on the excellence of your baseline." If your baseline is weak, your "gains" are illusions.

The Roadmap: From Video to Autonomous Agents

  • The L0-L3 Intelligence Hierarchy: Xie outlines a staircase toward AGI:
      • L0: Pure language models (blind to the world).
      • L1: Current multimodality (Show-and-Tell/VQA).
      • L2: Streaming event cognition (processing continuous visual streams).
      • L3: Spatial cognition (understanding the physical 3D space behind the pixels).
  • Downloading Humanity: The next data frontier isn't scraping the internet's text (already exhausted), but "downloading humanity"—capturing the 1st-person video streams that humans experience.
  • The "Squirrel" Benchmark: Following Rich Sutton, Xie argues that building the intelligence of a squirrel (capable of navigating, foraging, and social survival in a 3D world) is a "harder problem" than writing code or winning math olympiads.

Leadership and Culture: The LeCun Partnership

  • Yann LeCun as "Captain": LeCun serves as the Executive Chairman and "spiritual leader." Xie describes him as a "reality distortion field" leader who manages via vision rather than day-to-day micromanagement.
  • JEPA as an Ocean: Xie transitioned from "doubting JEPA" to "becoming JEPA," viewing the Joint Embedding Predictive Architecture not as a specific algorithm but as a vast "ocean" for a new cognitive system.
  • "The Normal One": Eschewing the "Special One" persona, Xie adopts Jürgen Klopp’s "Normal One" philosophy—acting as a "battery" to empower the 25-person "grassroots" team at AMI Labs to take on the tech giants.

***

Notable Quotes

"Silicon Valley is very LLM-pilled... Language is an opiate. It makes you feel happy because it's useful, but it’s a shortcut. If you keep taking it, you’ll never train your legs to run." — Saining Xie
"The goal of research isn't to create 'impact' in an aggressive sense; it's to be understood. If you understand something and write it down so others understand it too, you increase the total intelligence on Earth. That is never wrong." — Saining Xie
"Building the intelligence of a squirrel is the hard problem. Once you can build a squirrel that survives in the real world, things like writing code or going to the moon will be the easy parts." — Saining Xie (referencing Rich Sutton)
"A true world model doesn't need to be aligned with human words; it needs to be aligned with the causal laws of physics." — Saining Xie

But anyway, at the beginning I think we did a work called Deeply Supervised Nets. Mm. This work was actually me and another more senior PhD student completed it together in collaboration. And at this time, this was around 2013, 2014. And at this time, deep learning finally began to explode. But I think this was also a very interesting moment. Because actually many people didn't accept this. Especially many professors working in computer vision didn't even accept this. Everyone thought deep learning was still a kind of alchemy, still a black box. People trusted traditional machine learning theory more, trusting SVMs, or trusting some Bayesian theories. Right. Being able to pivot in time to do deep learning research... this now, looking back with the benefit of hindsight, is a no-brainer. You didn't need to make that choice; right, you should just do it. But at the time, making such a choice, I think, required some courage. So Professor Tu actually is another reason I admire him very, very much, and I was deeply affected by this one thing. That is to say, he actually pivoted very promptly. So this Deeply Supervised Nets was in this era our first deep learning work. Right. So this thing was actually simple; it was about how all of these neural networks previously were just a single stream, a long chain with your input and getting your output. And now Deeply Supervised Nets... but this robotics isn't simple robotics. Meaning you can now actually have multiple branches—that is, your neural network can actually have multiple exits. And at different exits, you can apply a supervision signal. In this way, the most direct benefit is you can not only from the signal at the far end do back propagation back to the early layers—back propagation—you don't need to do back propagation from the far end all the way to the beginning. You can actually from an intermediate node do back propagation. This way can partially solve the vanishing gradient problem. Mm. And this actually relates to what came later—for example, ResNet actually has some resemblance; it's actually or in that era everyone actually wanted to solve this problem. So Deeply Supervised Nets was a way to solve this problem. Actually this thing, though it was long ago, right, this was again 12 years ago, but I think research is like this. 12 years later, actually, some of our current papers are again using the same kind of design; sometimes we don't even realize it. I think this is very interesting. But let's not talk about 12 years later. Right. So my second paper was called Holistically-Nested Edge Detection, a work on edge detection. HED. Right. I think about this paper, I'm actually quite proud of it. Because frankly, it solved a research problem. It was both lucky and unlucky. The lucky part is this paper was a good paper. The unlucky part is once the problem was solved, nobody worked on it afterward, so nobody cited your paper. So it lost many citations. But... but this work is essentially a Deeply Supervised Nets DSN applied to image or edge detection, but it's actually a global, what we call pixel labeling, pixel-level annotation task implementation. Mm. And this also opened up many new ways of thinking for me, because I would discover that a neural network, each of its layers actually has implicit structure and information in it. Your neural network, again, has not only input and output; in between, there is a lot of information it represents, a so-called hierarchical, hierarchical structure of the world. For edge detection, it represents that your early layers output edges that are more so-called coarse, more coarse edges. Right, and the further up, the more refined your edges become. So, finally you can take all of these edges and fuse them together to get one that best approximates human perception—such an edge output result. I think this was actually also giving me a new understanding of deep learning. It's a very interesting, very interesting thing. You can think of it as a black box, but each part of this black box you can open up, connect some new inspiration, and reach some new goals. I think this was very enlightening for me. Mm. Right, and then... and at that time, right, I think—I think what ResNeXt wanted to convey is also about how we, by modifying the neural network architecture, learn a more scalable, right, a more extensible representation. Such a representation, this thing is also very interesting because this idea is very, very simple. It says originally, for example, my ResNet is just a serial network—right, just layer by layer by layer like this, conv layers. Now I can in parallel expand into several different groups, each group with its own small network. So you have networks within a large network, distributed in parallel with many small networks. Mm. Why is this interesting? Because in today's terms, this is MoE. Oh. So, at least on ImageNet at the time, we already saw a kind of scaling behavior. That is, the more groups you have, the more sparse your neural network becomes. And the more sparse your neural network, the wider it gets, but you can at the same FLOPs computation level get better results. It converges faster and your final results also improve. I think this resonates with what people are doing with MoE today; it aligns very well. "Does this work count as an extension of Kaiming's ResNet?" Yes, yes. So why is it called ResNeXt? "Let me explain to everyone what representation learning is." Good question. Right, this thing—I think I think the reason I like saying I am someone who does representation learning is because this is still hard to define. Mathematically speaking, you can think of representation learning as: you have data, right, 'x', and you now want to map it to a space, and now this space might have some properties. These properties—maybe these good properties may make it easier for you on downstream tasks to achieve better results. Right. So what you want to learn, from the initial data to this well-property space mapping function, this is what is called representation learning. And then this function is also not just a simple mapping; it might be a hierarchical mapping. And now, of course, this can be implemented in different ways. Now the mainstream implementation is to use a non-linear neural network to implement this function. Right, so I think this is a definition. But I just said I would be willing to say I myself am someone who does representation learning because I think this is a timeless title. Because this field develops too fast. And so I think initially, the discussion about self-supervised learning—including Yann, he's a big advocate. That is, he is very invested in self-supervised learning. He has this classic cake analogy. "This metaphor." Right. The base layer is the body of the cake, and this part must be Self-Supervised Learning. On top of that you can have Supervised Learning, right, this is the icing on the cake, the cream on your cake. And further on top is Reinforcement Learning; it's just the cherry on top, just a little cherry at the very top. Mm. Each layer of this cake is actually important, but they're not ranked by importance. Mm. If you don't have the cake's base, you can't get to intelligence relying only on the cherry on top. Mm. Right. So because we were at FAIR doing vision, we were actually paying attention to this very early. But the process of this research went like this: around 2015 and 2016, people already knew that self-supervised learning was actually a future for vision. So at that time, people would design all kinds of what we call pretext tasks, or proxy objective goals, some proxy tasks. What is self-supervised learning? I don't have a label to directly give you, unlike ImageNet, where I have 1000 classes and can directly train a supervised classifier and get a representation this way. In the old days, this is what everyone was doing through 1000 class labels. By the way, within these 1000 classes there are 200 different dog breeds. Even so, this is why ImageNet is so powerful, right? Even with that distribution, it can still let our neural networks learn good representations. I think this is extremely impressive. But people also see the limitations. Once everything is just Supervised Learning, there are many things you can't capture. Mm. Because what it learns — for example, we're sitting here now, we see these chairs, right? And we now have a lot of images of different chairs. Some chairs might be quite ordinary, like chairs in a studio like ours, or chairs in a home, or some designer chairs, or like an avocado chair, a chair shaped like an avocado. For supervised learning, you need to map all of this to a single label; this label is called "chair." So what your network has to learn, this mapping, is actually very, very difficult. And it's an infinite mapping. Mm. So it can only either memorize, just remember, recite all the chairs it's ever seen, or tell you it's a chair through what we call spurious correlations, some false correlations. For example, it may not look at the chair itself but look at the background behind the chair, or it thinks all chairs will be next to a table, so it uses that to make a decision boundary and says, "Hey, this is a chair." But this is not what we want. What we want to achieve is, from this very diverse visual knowledge, these visual observations, to gain some kind of common sense, some kind of intuition. Intuition, right, or some kind of common understanding. So this is why people initially wanted to do so-called Self-Supervised Learning or Unsupervised Learning. A common misconception back then was people saying we want to do Unsupervised Learning because labeling data is too hard and too expensive. We need to hire people to label, spending money and time. We don't want to do that. But that's just one very small part of the problem. The bigger issue is, in the eyes of computer vision researchers, everyone knew long ago that only through this path there's no way to give AI systems this kind of common sense. So in 2015 and 2016, everyone was very, very creative. That period was actually a quite creative era. People would design all kinds of crazy tasks. These tasks — for example, you take an image, rotate it 90 degrees, or 180 degrees, or 270 degrees. You don't give these images a label, but because you designed how to rotate these images, these images and their rotation angles can form a valid pretext task. You can predict how these rotated images were actually rotated. This becomes a so-called proxy task. Similar proxy tasks also include giving an image, converting it to grayscale, removing all its colors, but then using a neural network to reconstruct the original colors. Essentially, from a grayscale image, how do you predict the color of each object as it should be. Mm. And there are other similar examples, too many to count. Another example, one last one: let me give one more example. The so-called Context Encoder — you take an image, cut out a piece in the middle, make it white, and then train a neural network to fill in this empty part. Fill it in. The rationale behind all these pretext tasks is that humans can actually do this. The reason humans can do this, the reason humans know whether this image was rotated 90 or 180 degrees, or what color the butterfly or house in this image should be, or what information is missing in the middle — all these things are because humans have this common sense based on some understanding of the physical world. So they can guess these corrupted signals, these already lost signals, and how they should be reconstructed. The masked signals, right. But back then the problem was a hundred flowers blooming — all kinds of papers, mm, but none of them worked well. All the results were actually quite poor, all worse than ImageNet pre-training by roughly 15-20 percentage points. So people were making some progress, moving forward step by step, but the gap with what ImageNet could achieve through Supervised Learning on large-scale data with labels was still far, far better. The representation learned with labels was still superior. Right? So, we did something at that time, and this was done together with Kaiming. This architecture is called MoCo, Momentum Contrast, or momentum contrastive learning. Right. Even the Chinese name sounds interesting. Yes, momentum contrastive learning. I think you don't need to dig into the specific technical details because now much of it is no longer important. But in short, it was the first to take what's called contrastive learning as a framework and make it actually work as a paper. And what is contrastive learning? Also quite simple. We're now in this Representation Space. In this representation space, there are different points. These points may be the same object or completely different objects. For example, I have several images of this chair, right, and also some that may be tables, or images of cats or dogs. These images are all different, but in this space, we can measure their distances. We know all these different chairs — their images should be closer, their representations should be closer. But a chair and a cat should be farther apart. Mm-hmm. So this is the basic logic of contrastive learning. And this is actually not new. It's been done for many, many years. By the way, this early work was actually Yann who first worked with his students to do it. That's very interesting. Of course, the problem being solved wasn't directly Representation Learning, but some Metric Learning problems. This was around 2019, I think we gave contrastive learning some new meaning. But this didn't come out of nowhere. Actually, before that, the entire field was slowly moving in this direction, expanding. For example, there was a paper called CPC, and another paper called Memory Bank. These two papers were already moving in this direction — using contrastive learning to do self-supervised learning, having already taken several steps. Right, and then this is where I can't help but admire Kaiming's ability. I think this is also a moment that made me think, "Wow, what a top-tier researcher."
