And this, I think, truly deeply influenced the way I do research, and what I now tell my students about how research should be done. It's about increasing input. Increasing input. And I think there's actually a paradigm here. Mm, in this, this paradigm is also something Kaiming taught me. Right, he said actually all these ideas you can't just sit there and think up, because if you want to come up with an idea by just thinking, it's definitely not a good idea. There are really only a few possibilities. The first possibility: you're smarter than everyone else in the world, so you come up with an incredibly brilliant idea that no one else can think of. But I think the probability of this is extremely small. So the more likely two possibilities: first, while you're thinking of this idea, 100 people, 1000 people, 10,000 people in the world are thinking the same idea. So you'll have to compete with them, and your execution speed may not be faster than theirs. The second possibility: this is a very bad idea that others have already tried many times unsuccessfully. Mm. Then you probably don't need to try either. Mm. So I think Kaiming's greatest influence on me is he taught me how to find a research idea. Mm. How? I think this is a process of seeking. So now, when new students come in, I will tell everyone about a research cycle. Of course, I hope it could be longer, but in today's competitive environment, there might be at most 6 months. That is, from the beginning of 6 months, you need to start thinking about an idea, and then later you need to write this idea into a paper and publish it. This whole cycle is about 6 months. What does this process look like? You need to have a general direction, you need to know what you want to do. You can't know nothing at all — just saying "I want to do research" isn't enough. This can be achieved by talking with your advisor, or with your peers, discussing with your classmates, or through your own reading, developing some general direction, this directional understanding. But you must give yourself enough time and space to explore. And this exploration phase, I think, should last at least one to two months. What should you do during the exploration phase? The exploration phase — good question. What do you do during exploration? You can't just sit there thinking. What you need to explore is constantly hacking things, that is, you really have to be like a hacker, playing with things, messing around with things. Treat research like a game, like a toy to play with. Mm, this might involve, for example, working through formulas, reading more papers, finding some connections, of course, and perhaps more importantly, actually doing things, writing code. But when you're writing code, what you need to note is the code you write is not your initial starting idea or direction, but an exploration process. So the code you write might simply reproduce a baseline, take what someone else's paper is doing and reproduce it. And it might also be on the basis of this baseline to make some kind of extension. And the most important thing in all this is to find a signal. That is, it's still a bit like what you just said — all of this decision-making process is actually a quite disorderly exploration process. It's what we call stochastic gradient descent, right? This is a cornerstone of all machine learning, but it equally applies to research itself and to our lives. That is, in everyone's pursuit of their ultimate goal, they're all going through a stochastic gradient descent process. Mm. And I think research is the same. For you, the most important thing in research is not going from point A to point B. For example, A is an idea, B is a paper, but rather in this process, what kind of signal can you find? Your gradient, where exactly is your gradient? Right. So Kaiming's view is this gradient itself is the source of your real idea. When you've gone through constant exploration, tried many things, possibly unsuccessful, possibly successful, by the way, it doesn't have to be a successful experiment to give you this gradient. Sometimes a failed experiment gives you a larger gradient. Right? That is, as long as — the most feared thing is not knowing which direction to go. Mm. So a good result or a bad result are both good results. For research, a surprise, something surprising, such an observation, is always for a researcher the most joyful thing. Something unexpected that you observed. Right. You saw something unexpected. So he said, it's after this kind of exploration, in this process, that the ideas you discover are truly your own ideas. The idea you started with isn't your idea. That thing doesn't belong to you. The idea found in exploration is your own idea. And the research process is about finding your own idea. But this word, you need to see it belongs to — this thing is truly your own. Like heaven gave you an inspiration, injected it into your head. Maybe you're truly a genius, or maybe you're extremely lucky, God holding your hand wrote this formula. It can happen. But most of the time, most progress, even most work that has great influence on the field, I think still happens step by step. You can always trace back to find its starting point. So I also tell students, what's actually the worst kind of research? It's when you define a problem at the start, say this is my idea, and in the end publish a paper whose idea is exactly the same as what you started with. You didn't encounter any obstacles, you didn't encounter any difficulties. Why is it the worst? Because this shows your idea is a boring idea, and your published paper is a boring paper. Right. I think after many years of observation, this is indeed very, very accurate. So I think this is also why I tell students this — because people sometimes can't accept this fact. People always think, "I should start by thinking of a clever trick, then implement it, make it work, publish a paper, I've succeeded, and I move on to the next thing." But what this can give for personal accumulation is actually very, very limited. The exploration process is actually very difficult. Many people don't know how to explore. Exploration is very hard. And this is why all these papers in my view are nonlinear. This nonlinearity shows in two aspects. The first is your 6 months of time — by the 5th month, like I just told you, your mindset collapses. This ResNeXt story — on one hand, people hear, "Wow, you changed direction in the last month and made it work. That time period is so short, and you still managed to do it." It sounds unbelievable. But once you know this happens too often, you find there really is a pattern. You often go through this. I often go through this. Or rather, my best work always happens this way. So how do you maintain your mindset for the first 5 months? There's no way around it. You have to accept this fact, you have to be able to tell yourself this is a normal research process. Would you consider switching direction in the first 5 months? I might go for that boring idea. I think you would. And changing direction is actually very, very important. You must learn to pivot. Because I just said, the worst work is when your starting idea is the same idea as your ending idea. The best work is when you've gone all around, jumping here and there, taken a long, winding road, and only then arrived at this point. Mm. Though this road is very bumpy, from the final destination step by step you can always trace back to the very beginning. Only then can it be connected into a line. During the process, you can't. This is actually MIT professor Bill Freeman — he has a very classic plot, an illustration, this kind of graphic. He often talks about it when giving talks. So, this graphic has a horizontal axis and a vertical axis. The horizontal axis starts from a very poor work, a decent work, a very good work, an exceptionally impressive work. The vertical axis is the impact on your entire career. The impact of this paper on your career. So you can guess what this curve actually looks like. It's not a linear curve. It's not that a very poor work has a very bad career impact, and the best work or a fairly good work gives you a very good return, gradually increasing. It's not linear. It's saying basically, a very poor work actually won't hurt you much; nobody cares. Mm. No one will notice. A decent work — no one notices either. The gains it brings you are also small. Mm. I think this illustrates the research game's nonlinear characteristic. Mm. So is the highest point good or not? Of course it's good! That is, you only need to succeed just once in your lifetime. And I actually gave a talk about this at CVPR, I called it Research: The Infinite Game. This got quite a strong response from everyone. I think actually I rarely give these non-technical talks because this is more about philosophical thinking and some summaries. That one was actually quite good, and it also contained everything I talked about above. Because think about it, research as a career, a researcher as a profession — what is its true essence? It's not a chess player, it's not even a Winter Olympics athlete. Because for a chess player and an athlete, your final achievement depends on your worst step to some extent. You have to ensure every step, your moves must be correct. If you make even a small mistake in the middle, if you make a small error in chess, placed a piece wrong once, you've lost. Right? So this is a finite game. In this process, there are always winners and always losers. But a researcher is more like an inventor: you in your lifetime truly only need to succeed once. Mm. Like how Kaiming gave me the Diamond Sutra. I think he — because the Diamond Sutra says all things are like dreams, illusions, bubbles and shadows. Taking this a bit further, it's actually quite similar to certain ideas in Western philosophy. Quite similar, actually. For example, Kant's concept of the thing-in-itself, and then Schopenhauer's The World as Will and Representation. Right. What they're all trying to express — I don't know much about philosophy, I don't want to sound pretentious. But in my humble understanding, I think what they're all trying to discuss is: what you see is not the essence of the thing. What you see of the world is not its true substance. So when you're reading a paper, what matters is to break through the illusion the paper presents to you. Question what lies behind this paper. What kind of substantive essence does it actually contain? I think the source of researcher taste lies in whether people can truly set aside all these superficial appearances and then keep pursuing the path toward truth. Keep seeking. Mm. I think Kaiming does this best. If you think about this from a long-term perspective, the question is: what is the right way to guide how you choose a topic? What kind of things to work on? Right. This thing also connects to, while you're doing research, what exactly should each step involve. I think everything is consistent. And then I think one problem with not having good research taste is people might get caught up in these appearances. These appearances might be a paper's acceptance, or the kind of fame you mentioned from the outside world, or being able to get something done quickly. Getting the kind of momentary praise and adulation. Right. I think for Kaiming this is completely outside of his world model. He simply doesn't care, I think. But if you ask me to list out research taste as points a, b, c, d... That becomes pretty hard to articulate this thing because it involves so many things. Research itself, as I said, is also a creative process. It's also a writing process. From the writing side, by the way, Kaiming is also the person with the strongest writing ability. He also strongly encouraged us, saying, "Make sure to start writing early." This thing, very unfortunately, even now at my age, I still can't do it well like Kaiming. All his papers were finished a month before the deadline—at least that was the case at FAIR. Mm, meaning while everyone else was pulling all-nighters to meet the deadline and then feeling this huge sense of satisfaction, Kaiming, you know, was like a carefree free spirit. Having finished everything a month ago, he was polishing it over and over again. Watching all of you rush to meet your deadlines, I, in a very relaxed way, have already made this thing perfect. He finished everything a month in advance. Everything done. Meaning the paper was fully written—not just the results obtained, but the paper fully written. This is already a publishable, solid piece of work. So that means he had to start writing when? Two months before the deadline? And he only needed one month to write it? No, one month is a long time, right? Of course, he would keep writing afterward. During that month before the deadline, he would polish every table, every single word, every punctuation mark. For example, this habit also influenced me. For instance, I now have this OCD-like obsession that also came from my time with Kaiming, which is that in your paper, not a single line should have less than 60% filled with text. What does that mean? Meaning if you have a line and more than half of it is empty, it doesn't look good. You need to fill that line or have it filled roughly sixty to seventy percent. Then your paper looks more elegant, uniform. Oh, and now with every paper, I always ask all the students, "Look carefully if you have some trailing word." If people aren't paying attention, you'll end up with a word sitting alone on a line somewhere. It looks terrible. Understood? Mm. And also, when Kaiming thinks about this, his view is: "This paper is not for you to read; this paper is for others to read." So you need to care about how others experience it. Mm. How can you? A paper is just a vessel. How do I, through this vessel of knowledge, let people relatively smoothly get to the core of what you want to express? This communication interface needs to be pleasing to the eye. That's a great way to put it, right? The communication interface must be pleasing to the eye. So you can't let your paper look too bad, right? You have to get the details right. So all of this, you can consider it a kind of research taste, but I think this is actually something more general—a kind of aesthetic toward life or toward everything in the universe. Mm. I think these things are all connected, right? This is also why we care so much about our own papers being as unique as possible, having our own distinctiveness. We can have our own webpage design, we'll record our own videos. Record videos? But there are many people who wonder why you bother with all this. This stuff has nothing to do with research. Isn't this just a distraction? Why spend extra energy polishing all this? Are you just doing this for hype and marketing? I hope people don't think that, because I think having your own style is actually very important. Mm. And then this is also why all of our papers use a consistent template. And from a code perspective—I care a lot about these things. From your code perspective, what I call Minimal Description Length. Your code is actually quite important; it can reflect some things. If your code is short and can achieve the same purpose, then your method will typically be better than one that requires thousands of lines of code, an extremely complex system. Even if it can do the same thing, but the former, this more elegant solution, the simpler solution, is always better. I think this is also a kind of research taste in a sense. So we found, "Hey, this thing is both simple and it works, and scalable and efficient." So it seems like this thing is the direction we should be pursuing. In a really difficult position, right? Why? Mainly because, first, not enough resources. Let me give a simple example. Your autoencoder, your representation layer will now become very high-dimensional because it's a representation now. It's not the original simple pixel-level representation, nor is it a low-dimensional VAE-type representation." It's a high-dimensional representation. You want to do denoising and image generation on this high-dimensional representation. This is actually a very difficult thing, right? And a student asked at the time, "This dimension is too high; it might not necessarily be a good thing." And then it might make our learning system more complex or make training harder. First of all, our results are completely the opposite conclusion, but Professor Ma Yi got very excited. He stood up and said, "I want to sincerely tell everyone you must not be afraid of high dimensions." High dimensionality is, in all machine learning, an extremely important cornerstone. Including whether in previous so-called kernel learning methods, kernel methods, or why in a Transformer we need to have an Up Projection Layer, right? You need to have a low-dimensional vector coming in and then turning it into a 4 times larger, 4 times wider Fully Connected layer. And then all these things are all telling us the following fact: that in a high-dimensional space, many problems that couldn't be solved in low-dimensional space can now be solved. Many types of information that didn't exist in low-dimensional space can now exist, and you'll also have better efficiency. This is traditional machine learning theory. Why you need to do—after increasing dimensions, making things, making your data points linearly separable. All the same logic. But I feel very encouraged in that you should not be afraid of high dimensions. I think these are very good words because many times people feel afraid, right? Feel afraid not just of high-dimensional representation, but also afraid of escaping from some current local optimum. Meaning, right now many things we've done before were all done to jump out of this local optimum, mm-hmm. Like VAE is the current era's local optimum. In strict definitional terms, a world model means you're now given a system or the state of an environment. This environmental state might be, for example, you can think of it as the state at the current moment. But a world model doesn't necessarily just make temporal predictions, but let's not worry about that for now. Anyway, you first have a system or an environment. You have a state S_t, right? And you have an intervention or action—let's call it a_t at the current moment. You apply an action to this system. You now hope to learn a predictive function or transition function F. So that it can take your action together with your current state, this environmental state, to predict the next state, right? The state at the next moment. So this is the most basic, general kind of definition of a world model. And this definition itself is actually incredibly straightforward or even somewhat trivial, because this isn't a new concept. Actually, back in 1943 there was this physiologist called Kenneth Craik, a Scottish philosopher and psychologist, mm-hmm, who first proposed this concept. He said humans have in their minds such a world model. This world model can tell us when we take some action, what consequences will follow, mm-hmm. Because we can predict our actions, the consequences our actions bring. So this can guide us in what kind of action to take and what kind of decision to make. If I know that putting my hand in a fire will hurt, then I won't put my hand in the fire. This thing—this kind of prediction structure—is also from the past. Including control theory in the 1960s and 70s—how everyone would put a lunar probe to the moon or send it to wherever, right? And then everyone actually needs to be based on such a control system. For example, a classic algorithm called Model Predictive Control. This also involves a Model, but this Model is actually also a kind of World Model. This algorithm is actually very, very simple, meaning you now need to decide what control signal exactly I should apply to this system to enable it to complete a predetermined task, mm-hmm, right? And what I need to do is at the current moment roll out through my model to continuously output the next k steps of actions. An action sequence—meaning I need to output my next action sequence, a sequence of actions. And through this action sequence, use my Model to get the next step or the state at each step. And finally I'll also have some kind of cost function, a metric function, which tells me after I execute this action sequence how far I am from my ultimate goal, how far the distance is. So this algorithm is very simple. You continuously sample your action sequence, then jump back to the first step and find the action sequence with the lowest cost. Execute its first step, then repeatedly iterate to do this action and roll out the next action sequence. The model doesn't need many training parameters because you don't need to remember everything. If you want to do video generation, that's a different story. But you don't need to remember everything—all the subtle details in the world that you can see. It doesn't need to memorize all this knowledge. It needs good understanding capability to filter information, processing and filtering out information. This is actually extremely high bandwidth. This bandwidth might reach, say, 1 billion bits per second—in the range of 100 million to 1 billion. Mm-hmm. But when we're talking right now, the bandwidth is extremely low. The bandwidth is only ten to one hundred bits per second. Mm-hmm. So what's actually happening? Right? What kind of model is our brain that, at twenty watts of power, takes in one billion bits per second of information through our eyes and all kinds of sensory inputs, and converts it into 10 bits per second of behavioral output? This is the World Model itself. It filters out large amounts of useless information and noise, right? There's a lot of redundancy. It knows what's important and what's not important. And then—but later with JEPA, for example, including me, to give a simple example, recently there was a paper called LeJEPA. And with a very rigorous proof, they showed if you want a good representation, if you want this representation to be agnostic to your downstream task, then it must be an isotropic Gaussian distribution. This is a bit technical. Essentially, it means it's a characterization of a certain property of representations. And I found this actually has merit. Truly becoming JEPA is because I feel JEPA is not a model. JEPA is not a specific algorithm. JEPA is a complete cognitive architecture. It's a cognitive system. This in Yann's 2022 paper is what he wrote about. So in my view, this cognitive system is a path to intelligence—a universal intelligent agent's... in my current view, a very reasonable path. Well, that's not a great way to end. I'm going to start complaining again. Right, go ahead. You complain. I say, let go of Wittgenstein means you shouldn't—people shouldn't take Wittgenstein and really stretch him, using it as a language boundary meaning the limits of my world, and use that quote as endorsement for LLMs or linguistic determinism. So that's completely absurd. And likewise, there are other quotes, like people citing Feynman. Feynman said what I cannot create I do not understand. This being used to endorse unified models—I think both of these things are really unacceptable to me. What's the first thing? The first is Wittgenstein, right? When he spoke of the limits of language as the limits of my world, there were strong preconditions in his *Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus*. What he discussed in the *Tractatus* was that the language he referred to targets what can be captured in propositions—the limits of the world that can be described. And this does not represent the entirety of what we call the world. So first, the language he spoke of and the world he spoke of are already different from the language in today's LLMs and the world it refers to. Second, in his later period, Wittgenstein had completely overturned his earlier entire philosophical system. He later stopped saying that, and what he talked about instead was language is actually a game, the so-called concept of language games—meaning language itself has no inherent meaning. These symbols themselves have no meaning. The reason they acquire meaning is because they are connected to real-world practice and engaged with it. Mm-hmm. And this is very much the world model view. That is, we're not saying that language can perfectly represent the entire world; what we're saying is that the world's practice, the world's actions, determine the game of language, its intension and extension. Mm-hmm. Again, I don't understand philosophy; I don't understand Wittgenstein either. But I just don't like seeing in people's papers opening with a pulled quote. I think that doesn't fit my aesthetic sensibilities. The Feynman quote is the same. Mm-hmm. He said what I cannot create I do not understand. That quote itself is not wrong. But the "create" and "understand" he's referring to mean, for example, we have a world, we want to understand this world, we want to transform this world, we want to understand the world through transforming it. Whatever the things he was talking about are still within a real, concrete world requiring some kind of action. Mm-hmm. Even when you're in class and you go and make a PowerPoint, you're still engaged in a process of creation. But now many people take this quote and use it to make this kind of endorsement for some simple unified system. That's logically untenable too. We can't simply reduce creation to a diffusion model its backpropagation loss. That's completely absurd. Mm-hmm, right? So I don't know. President Trump signs executive order launching anti-fraud task force headed by JD Vance

Fox News · 2026-03-16
Donald Trump (President of the United States) JD Vance (Vice President of the United States) Andrew Ferguson (Commissioner, FTC)
President Trump signed an executive order establishing a "whole-of-government" anti-fraud task force led by Vice President JD Vance to reclaim billions in misappropriated federal funds while providing a status update on the ongoing military decimatio...
TL;DR President Trump signed an executive order establishing a "whole-of-government" anti-fraud task force led by Vice President JD Vance to reclaim billions in misappropriated federal funds while providing a status update on the ongoing military decimation of Iran's nuclear and...

TL;DR

President Trump signed an executive order establishing a "whole-of-government" anti-fraud task force led by Vice President JD Vance to reclaim billions in misappropriated federal funds while providing a status update on the ongoing military decimation of Iran's nuclear and conventional capabilities.

Launch of the National Anti-Fraud Task Force

  • Leadership and Structure: The task force will be chaired by Vice President JD Vance, with FTC Commissioner Andrew Ferguson serving as Vice Chairman.
  • Fiscal Objective: Root out fraud in federally funded programs to potentially recover "hundreds of billions" of dollars. President Trump suggested that eliminating this fraud alone could balance the national budget.
  • Whole-of-Government Approach:
      • Inter-agency Cooperation: The order mandates that agencies like the Treasury, DOJ, and HHS share data to identify financial and Medicaid fraud that previously went undetected due to siloed information.
      • Targeted Investigations: Initial focus includes "blue state" fraud, specifically highlighting a $19 billion scam in Minnesota involving the Somali community and autism benefit programs.
  • Devolution of Oversight: The administration plans to shift oversight of local entities, such as nursing homes and nurseries, from the federal government back to the states to reduce bureaucratic bloat and vulnerability to theft.

Immigration and Taxpayer "Extraction"

  • Benefit Misuse: Stephen Miller reported that internal ICE surveys show significant numbers of illegal immigrants are voluntarily reporting enrollment in Medicaid or receiving free hospital care billed to taxpayers.
  • Systemic Fraud: The administration alleges that previous leadership allowed a system to exist that funnels "trillions of dollars" to migrants who do not belong in the country.
  • Enforcement Actions: JD Vance noted that the task force will utilize DOJ resources to criminally prosecute fraudsters, particularly those siphoning benefits intended for American citizens with disabilities.

Military Conflict and "Epic Fury" in Iran

  • Strategic Objective: Trump defended the "Epic Fury" campaign as a necessary "excursion" to prevent Iran from obtaining nuclear weapons, which he claimed would lead to the incineration of Israel and World War III.
  • Operational Status:
      • Military Decimation: The President claimed U.S. forces have destroyed Iran’s navy, air force, anti-aircraft defenses, and multiple layers of leadership.
      • Nuclear Infrastructure: B-2 bombers were utilized to strike hardened nuclear sites and "extinguish" their capacity.
  • Criticism of Allies: Trump expressed deep disappointment in NATO and UK leadership (Prime Minister Keir Starmer) for failing to provide military support, such as mine sweepers, until after the U.S. had already secured the region.
  • Geopolitical Shifts: Trump signaled that Cuba is "next" on the agenda, describing it as a "failed nation" that the U.S. intends to "free" or "take" in some capacity.

Domestic Policy and "Save America Act"

  • Voter Integrity: The administration is pushing for the Senate to pass the Save America Act, which focuses on three pillars: mandatory Voter ID with a photo, proof of citizenship, and an end to mail-in ballots.
  • Social Mandates: The President intends to attach riders to the legislation that would ban "transgender surgery for children" and prohibit biological men from participating in women’s sports.
  • Partisan Unity: Trump criticized the lack of Republican unity compared to Democrats, whom he described as "sticking together like glue" even on "horrible policy."

Notable Quotes

"If we found half of the fraud that's taking place in this country... we would have much more than a balanced budget. That's the kind of numbers you're talking about. The theft is incredible." — President Donald Trump
"What this executive order does is force the entire apparatus of the federal government to do two things: stop the fraud on the American taxpayer and make sure that the benefits that ought by right go to American citizens go to American citizens and not to fraudsters." — Vice President JD Vance
"The extraction of wealth from American taxpayers to people who don't belong here is the primary cause of the national debt and this is the first ever effort to shut that down." — Stephen Miller
"I always said, you know the problem with NATO is we'll always be there for them but they'll never be there for us... I don't need your aircraft carriers after we've already won." — President Donald Trump

Anduril's Palmer Luckey on AI, nukes, and the war in Iran | The Axios Show

Axios(71.6K) ·25:47 ·7.2K views · 2026-03-16
Palmer Luckey (Founder, Anduril) Colin Demarest (Defense Reporter, Axios)
Anduril Industries is disrupting the traditional defense model by using private capital to rapidly develop autonomous systems, with founder Palmer Luckey advocating for the U.S.
TL;DR Anduril Industries is disrupting the traditional defense model by using private capital to rapidly develop autonomous systems, with founder Palmer Luckey advocating for the U.S. to transition from "world’s police" to the "world’s gun store" through high-scale, AI-driven...

TL;DR

Anduril Industries is disrupting the traditional defense model by using private capital to rapidly develop autonomous systems, with founder Palmer Luckey advocating for the U.S. to transition from "world’s police" to the "world’s gun store" through high-scale, AI-driven weaponry.

Company Culture and Disruptive Model

  • The "Effective" Mandate: Luckey defines Anduril’s culture through competence and speed, employing a workforce that is 20% veterans to ensure tools are designed for real-world deployment.
  • Financial Model: Unlike traditional "cost-plus" contractors, Anduril spends its own R&D money to build products. This allows them to continue development during political or budgetary gridlock that would typically halt "entrenched primes" like Lockheed or Boeing.
  • Recruitment Strategy: Luckey acknowledges his "caricature" persona serves as a recruiting engine, attracting talent that aligns with the company’s mission while "repelling the wrong people."

Product Development and "Arsenal One"

  • Flagship Programs:
      • Fury: An autonomous fighter jet for the Air Force; Luckey considers this the company’s "coolest" achievement for successfully competing against major defense primes.
      • Ghost Shark: Large-scale robotic submarines developed for Australia, with the first unit delivered a year ahead of schedule.
      • Barracuda: Modular cruise missiles designed for high-volume production.
  • Production Scaling: The "Arsenal One" facility—a five-million-square-foot production hub—is set to go live in Ohio within weeks. It is designed to mass-produce missiles and aircraft for the Air Force, Army, Marine Corps, and SOCOM.
  • The Subterranean Frontier: Luckey identifies the "crust of the earth" as the next major warfighting domain, developing prototypes for subterranean kinetic and electronic systems.

Geopolitics and the Ethics of Warfare

  • The "World's Gun Store": Luckey argues the U.S. no longer has the political will for "boots on the ground" campaigns. He suggests providing allies with advanced tools to defend themselves as the most viable strategic path.
  • Nuclear and Chemical Weapons:
      • Nuclear Deterrent: Luckey stated he would "absolutely" build fission and fusion weapons, calling them the most stabilizing force in history for preventing total war.
      • Chemical Agents: He advocates for the military use of non-lethal agents like pepper spray and tear gas, which are currently restricted by chemical weapon prohibitions.
  • Civilian Oversight: Luckey strongly opposes tech companies (like Anthropic or Google) setting their own limits on military AI usage, arguing that the use of force must remain accountable to elected civilian leadership, not private executives.

The AI Race and Policy

  • U.S. vs. China: While the U.S. maintains a lead in AI model quality, China is moving faster in "fielding" AI across its military and surveillance apparatus due to structural authoritarian advantages.
  • Domestic Policy: Luckey credited California Governor Gavin Newsom for "saving Anduril" by vetoing a bill that would have prohibited the use of AI in weapons, which Luckey claims would have crippled U.S. military capability.
  • Department of War: Luckey praised the current Pentagon leadership for leveraging AI effectively in operations (notably in the Middle East) and for providing "early, honest feedback" to contractors via public and private channels.

***

Notable Quotes

"Anduril is a defense product company that spends its own money designing, developing and shipping tools that will save taxpayers hundreds of billions of dollars a year as we make tens of billions of dollars a year someday." — Palmer Luckey
"The United States probably needs to transition from being the world's police to being the world's gun store. We need to give the people that work with us... the tools they need to defend themselves." — Palmer Luckey
"You are giving more power to private companies, executives making billions off taxpayers, than the President of the United States. They could just unilaterally pull the keys on stuff... Our military has to be accountable to our civilian elected leadership, not corporations." — Palmer Luckey
Palmer Luckey, good to see you again and welcome to the Axios Show. Thank you for having us on. You are the founder and figurehead of Anduril Industries, among the splashiest, fastest-growing, most pro-autonomy and polarizing defense contractors on the planet, I would hazard. Wow, seven descriptors. Yes, I'm big on adjectives. You have your haters and you have your hot takes, so I'd like to lean into both if you're game. I am always game to lean into the things that make me polarizing. Fantastic. Describe for me Anduril in one sentence, one quick sentence. Anduril is a defense product company that spends its own money designing, developing and shipping tools that will save taxpayers hundreds of billions of dollars a year as we make tens of billions of dollars a year someday. Someday. We're not there yet. Describe to me the culture in one word. Effective. Why? Almost everything that we have here is oriented around what type of competence will lead to actually delivering things that actually work with the customer on a timeline that is relevant. We need to build tools that are effective and sometimes something needs to come now rather than later to be effective. Sometimes it needs to be higher capability to be effective or not. Sometimes if it's not cheap enough to buy in scale that matters, then it's not effective. We're about 20% veterans here. People are very close to the customers, they're very close to the deployments and they understand, I think a lot better than a lot of other companies, how the military is truly using our tools. We have people who are forward-deployed, we have people who are overseas in the Middle East right now. We've had people who are in Ukraine since the second week of the war and so I think we have a very good understanding of what it takes to be effective with our customers. And if we don't, we develop it. Like if we go and we learn that we are screwing it up, we put our own resources into fixing it. To zoom out on effective, where in the world have you been effective? Is that Ukraine with drones, is that Australia with Ghost Shark, is that the Middle East? So we've been effective in... we've been effective in a lot of places and of course we've had bouts where it was ineffective. You know, like I'd say we've been very effective in the Middle East right now. We've also had challenges in the past and then we've surged resources to get that fixed, which is one of the reasons that things are working right now. But I think we've been effective in Ukraine. Australia, we're just beginning to deliver there, right? We've built a factory there, we've hired a large team there, we've developed this Ghost Shark program with them building robotic submarines at a very large scale very quickly. Your competition in the defense contractor world includes the entrenched primes, the Lockheeds, the Northrops, the Boeings. If I'm a Pentagon buyer, why do I buy Anduril versus those guys? So it depends on what you're doing, but in general the sense that a lot of people have in the Pentagon is that Anduril is a company that is going to move very, very quickly. We're going to bring a lot of our own resources to bear on the project and that even when programs have run into political problems, because we're willing to spend our own money as a product company continuing to keep things going, there's a number of programs that have survived political breaks that would have killed a lot of traditional cost-plus programs. And so I think a lot of the people in the program offices in the Pentagon that are making decisions, whether they're selecting Anduril or let's say keeping Anduril in the mix... you know, it's not always we're going to award to Anduril, it's we're going to select two or three companies to work on this. I think the reason we stay in that basket is they know that we'll move quickly, they know we'll put our own money in, they know that even when we run into political challenges we're not going to do a stop-work order. We're not going to just shut down our lines; we're going to keep moving and hope that we come out the other side. India’s Fastest Growing AI Startup

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·39:33 ·7.3K views · 2026-03-16
Mukund Jha (Founder, Emergent) Madhav Jha (Founder, Emergent)
Emergent is one of Y Combinator’s fastest-growing startups, enabling non-technical users to build and deploy over 7 million production-ready apps in eight months through a powerful multi-agent AI system that focuses on autonomous verification and integrated infrastructure.
TL;DR Emergent is one of Y Combinator’s fastest-growing startups, enabling non-technical users to build and deploy over 7 million production-ready apps in eight months through a powerful multi-agent AI system that focuses on autonomous verification and integrated infrastructure....

TL;DR

Emergent is one of Y Combinator’s fastest-growing startups, enabling non-technical users to build and deploy over 7 million production-ready apps in eight months through a powerful multi-agent AI system that focuses on autonomous verification and integrated infrastructure.

Rapid Growth and Strategic Pivot

  • Founding Pedigree: Founders Mukund and Madhav Jha (twin brothers) bring deep technical backgrounds from Google, Amazon Deep Learning, and Dunzo (India's leading quick-commerce company).
  • The "Verification" Insight: Originally applied to YC with a software testing agent. They realized that solving verification (the ability for an AI to test and fix its own work) was the key to unlocking general coding agents.
  • Explosive Metrics:
      • App Creation: 7 million apps built in 8 months; the user base doubled in the last 45 days.
      • Global Reach: Users in over 190 countries; 70-80% of the audience is based in the U.S. and Europe.
  • Pivot to Non-Technical Users: While the platform began as a research-heavy tool for engineers, 80% of current users have zero programming knowledge, using "vibe-coding" to ship real business applications.

Technical Architecture and "Agent Experience" (AX)

  • Custom Infrastructure: Unlike competitors who use third-party sandboxes, Emergent built its own Kubernetes-based container tech stack.
      • Consistency: Using the same infrastructure for build-time and deploy-time prevents common deployment errors.
      • Feedback Loops: In-house infra allows the agent to receive rapid, autonomous feedback, which is critical for the verification loop.
  • Multi-Agent Orchestration: The system uses a "Main Agent" for routines and delegates specialized tasks (testing, design, API integration) to "Sub-Agents."
  • Continual Learning and Memory: Emergent uses a long-term memory system where agents learn across user sessions. If an agent struggles with a specific API integration one week, it uses those "trajectories" to perform better in future sessions.
  • Tech Stack Choice: They utilize a Python backend and React frontend server-client architecture to support complex needs like asynchronous video processing and background jobs.

Operational Philosophy and Lean Hiring

  • The Bangalore-SF Split: The 12-person engineering team is primarily based in Bangalore, with a small presence in San Francisco.
  • High-Bar Hiring: The team focuses on "ownership" and "problem-solving," specifically targeting top-tier talent like Top 100 IIT rankers from India.
  • Extreme Ownership: Despite their growth, the team remains lean. A single person manages the memory system, while two people manage a deployment stack comparable to Vercel.
  • Direct Customer Empathy: Every employee, including top engineers, performs customer support. They believe engineers must "empathize with the agent" to understand where the AI struggles.

The Future of SaaS and "Agentic" Software

  • The "Death of SaaS" Headwind: Traditional SaaS companies face two threats:

1. Workflows being consumed by agents rather than UI.

2. Users building highly customized internal tools (e.g., Emergent replaced its own Asana subscription with an internally-built "Asana clone").

  • Agentic Evolution: Approximately 20% of apps built on Emergent are now agentic, meaning they have AI agents embedded within them to run autonomous workflows.
  • Long-Horizon Tasks: Future development is focused on "agent swarms" and "overseeing agents" that can manage tasks lasting 24 hours or longer.
  • Democratizing Innovation: The platform is enabling a "Cambrian explosion" of niche software, such as "EquiMind," an app created by a clinical psychologist for horse riders—a product too niche for traditional dev shops but viable via AI.

So what you do is hey let the main agent, the driving agent, handle the the main routine. But any delegated task that you want to delegate you delegate to a sub-agent. Be it like testing, be it like hey I want to do a design search or I want to do like you know integration search. Like how do I integrate this unique API. and along the way when we were like finding doing all of this, we were able to figure out okay all the trajectories that we are generating, we can kind of aggregate over time and like sort of build an a long-term memory for the agent. Which is very unique in the sense that your agent learns not just from your own session, it learns across the sessions. This is something I would say is one variant of continual learning that people are like interested in now. you would have noticed that people are interested in skills like people create like new skills and the there is a new benchmark called Skills-bench which shows like agent with skills outperform agent without skills and interestingly like those skills cannot be generated by agent themselves. Like if you generate those skills by agents they don't like match up to the performance. So we were able to do it in a way where the skills get auto-ha- you know sort of generated based on previous trajectories and we run it through a CI/CD process and then add it to the long-term memory. so all of that like compounds for us right. So if if your agents were struggling to do a calendar integration three weeks ago, today it is no longer struggling thanks to the the previous session where it was able to make it happen. Building AI for better healthcare — the OpenAI Podcast Ep. 14

OpenAI(1.9M) ·30:55 ·3.7K views · 2026-03-16
Nate Gross (Head of Health, OpenAI) Karan Singhal (Health AI Research Lead, OpenAI)
OpenAI is developing specialized AI foundations for healthcare by integrating physician feedback into every stage of model training, aiming to transform the industry from a reactive system into a proactive, accessible "safety net" for both clinicians and patients.
TL;DR OpenAI is developing specialized AI foundations for healthcare by integrating physician feedback into every stage of model training, aiming to transform the industry from a reactive system into a proactive, accessible "safety net" for both clinicians and patients. ...

TL;DR

OpenAI is developing specialized AI foundations for healthcare by integrating physician feedback into every stage of model training, aiming to transform the industry from a reactive system into a proactive, accessible "safety net" for both clinicians and patients.

Strategy for Health-Specific AI Development

  • Consumer Demand: Approximately 1 in 4 ChatGPT users (40 million people per day) currently use the platform for health-related queries, necessitating a dedicated health strategy.
  • The ChatGPT Health Foundation: A specialized framework designed to provide secure, context-aware interactions.
      • Data Privacy: Uses encrypted "one-way valves" to protect conversations; OpenAI explicitly does not train its models on user healthcare data.
      • Context Retention: Unlike search engines that have "amnesia," the health foundation allows patients to ground AI responses in their own medical history and personal context.
  • The Three-Tiered Mission: Dr. Nate Gross defines the team's objectives as:
      • Raise the Floor: Making medical expertise accessible to everyone globally.
      • Sweep the Floor: Automating administrative and bureaucratic burdens to give doctors more time with patients.
      • Raise the Ceiling: Accelerating medical discovery and achieving "transformative" outcomes that exceed current human capabilities.

Rigorous Training and "HealthBench" Evaluation

  • Physician Collaboration: OpenAI worked with a cohort of 250 physicians to generate data and evaluate model performance across 5,000 simulated clinical conversations.
  • Multidimensional Rubrics: The models are evaluated using "HealthBench," which measures approximately 49,000 dimensions of performance, including:
      • Seeking Context: Training the model to ask follow-up questions (e.g., if a user says "it burns") rather than providing immediate, potentially unsafe advice.
      • Adaptive Literacy: The ability to tailor responses based on the user’s role, such as explaining a condition differently to a pharmacist in Kenya versus a patient with a 3rd-grade literacy level.
      • Uncertainty Calibration: Reducing "overconfident hallucinations" by training the model to admit when it does not know an answer and suggesting specific clinical follow-ups.
  • Deep Integration: Unlike other models that focus on passing medical board exams, OpenAI integrates health-specific data into every phase: pre-training, mid-training, and post-training.

Clinical Utility and Real-World Impact

  • Clinical Co-pilot Study: In a partnership with Panda Health involving 20+ clinics in Nairobi, OpenAI deployed an AI "safety net" to monitor electronic health records.
      • Error Reduction: The study showed a statistically significant reduction in diagnostic and treatment errors when the AI flagged concerning data for clinicians.
  • Multimodal Integration: The future of the platform involves "zero-to-one capabilities" where models analyze decades of history, lab tests, and wearable data (rings, watches, biosensors) to predict health trends a human might miss.
  • Unified AI Layers: OpenAI aims to use the API to connect fragmented, siloed healthcare systems—bridging the gap between analog, digital, structured, and unstructured data.

Emerging "Aha" Moments in Development

  • Medical Breakthroughs: The research team is seeing instances where AI identifies new, direct clinical values for medications that have been "sitting on a shelf" for years.
  • The "Waymo" Effect: Karan Singhal notes a shift in sentiment where clinicians are beginning to feel it is "dangerous" to work *without* AI, comparing it to the safety of an autonomous vehicle compared to a human driver.
  • Solving "Miracle Cases": The technology is increasingly being used to solve "unsolved diagnoses" for patients who have struggled within the traditional medical system for years.

Notable Quotes

"Healthcare today... is fragmented, care is missed left and right... The system is more reactive than it is proactive today. That leads to tremendous challenges... leaving people behind in situations when they could be thriving."

— Dr. Nate Gross

"I want everybody, whether they're a patient or a health professional, to think about the ways in which as a patient you want to feel safer having this, as a health professional you want this to be a safety net for the decisions that you're making."

— Karan Singhal

"One of the most important things is that the model can be trained to better know when it doesn't know and say that."

— Dr. Nate Gross

"They actually had a lot of hesitance around running another study because that would have involved having some group of clinicians using AI and some group of clinicians not using AI. They actually felt that it was dangerous to have a group of clinicians not using the AI."

— Karan Singhal (referencing the Nairobi clinical study)

That's like a range of things from, "Are you tailoring your response to a lay person versus a more technical health professional?" "Are you thinking about the ways in which you should seek context first before providing an initial response?" The models used to be significantly, are much better today at kind of seeking context when needed because users are typing in much less than the models often need to be able to provide information that's most helpful. It burns. Exactly. If a user types in "it burns," how do you think about the right way to provide information? You can provide some initial information potentially based on an impression you might have of what the user might be saying, but the most helpful thing to do in that situation and the safest thing to do in that situation is actually to ask for more context. Compare the one-size-fits-all handouts that people get when they visit the doctor today to a model that can respond differently when it knows you're an oncologist versus a primary care doctor versus a pharmacist in Kenya versus a patient at the 12th-grade literacy level or the 3rd-grade literacy level. This is extremely important for not only making sure that accuracy and impact is maximized, but also just to make sure that everyone can maximally participate in their own care on the patient side. This was a study where we worked with these 20-plus clinics in Nairobi and actually thought about the ways in which we can deploy a safety net for clinicians in that context which is basically monitoring the things that they type into their electronic health record and only interrupting their flow when there's something potentially concerning that's going on or a potential error. That's another frontier that we are really excited about and would love to see more from our partners. Nate, what do you think the challenges are going to be? I'll start with talking through some of the challenges that exist on the professional side. Each day when healthcare professionals use AI, they're looking for the ability to trust what they're seeing in the answer. A lot of our recent work has been making sure that answers that the AI is providing are not just grounded in what the model was training on, but are grounded in the latest medical literature, the latest guidelines, and sometimes the latest guidance from their own institution or their own region. Some conditions are treated differently in different areas of the country. Other times different care settings have different levels of resources, different levels of specialists and additional services on hand, and it can be helpful as a healthcare professional to be able to quickly navigate that and come up with completely personalized care plans. Building connectivity within ChatGPT to not only be HIPAA aligned and be used in these secure environments, but also be able to combine sensitive information with the latest medical knowledge I think is a great path that we've started down and something that will continue to keep trust as the top priority between how healthcare professionals engage with AI. One of the other challenges is that the systems themselves in healthcare are quite siloed, both at an organization level, but also at the tools that have to be used within each organization. AI thus far has been deployed on a point-solution basis in the technology industry, but increasingly the connectivity is becoming available to connect the dots between the hundreds of different systems, some analog, some digital, some structured, some unstructured, many decentralized, many not on the cloud. Being able to connect all of those through unified AI layers to actually make sure that patients and information isn't falling through the cracks and that the connectivity can be maximized to actually bring the greatest amount of impact, that's hard in healthcare and it's certainly not something that we can say is solved. But with many of our recent products ranging from ChatGPT for Healthcare and its connectivity to apps and connectors to the OpenAI API for Healthcare to our Frontier Foundation for models and agents, we think increasingly there's going to be an opportunity to really accelerate what is possible within the healthcare system and what agents can achieve. Part of this seems like it's very collaborative, working with the healthcare industry. It's extremely important that all of the healthcare system has an equal chance to contribute and engage nationally and internationally with providing the context that will help empower patients to receive the best possible answers from ChatGPT. On the electronic health record side, this means working with the government and Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, adopting national standards for electronic health record syncing so that patients in just a few taps are able to bring in their context in consented ways. It's being able to tap into existing standards like mobile phones and the most popular consumer health products and the most popular biosensors and wearables to make sure again in just one or two taps patients are able to not only bring in that information but leverage it in thoughtful ways, in ways that may not have been possible without the combined set of data that can exist in this sort of ecosystem. For instance, being able to reference your recent exercise activity when making a plan of how to spend your evening or being able to even do things as simple as reference your overnight sleep and stress when your agent is helping you set your calendar for the next day and what tasks you may take on first. It's very exciting. You know, I have a smart ring, a watch, whatever, but I get this data and all I kind of have in my apps are rings to look at and go like, "Okay, I guess it's doing something." Being able to plug into ChatGPT has been fantastic because now I'm able to ask those kinds of questions. But it's very exciting what you talk about too is if you get a plan from your doctor, suggestions, is literally say, "Hey, I didn't walk enough yesterday, what should I do today?" I've had it be really good at menu planning, like literally go on this menu, tell me what to order and whatnot. And so you're saying we're just going to get more of that and much better. Yeah, and that's why our partnerships I believe are so important because in these instances, ChatGPT doesn't replace the incredible technology that our partners are building to go deep on health insights for a particular wearable. But our surface area, our opportunity to bring in that health information can now extend to the many different ways people use ChatGPT, such as what they're going to cook for dinner or how they're going to plan their afternoon. Sometimes I think of two patients and one patient has to navigate the healthcare system by themselves, and the other patient maybe has a spouse come with them, and that spouse has a clipboard and used to work as a healthcare professional and is very attentive if not neurotic and can follow up on details and is connected to your personal calendar. The best aspects of that with consent for the patients that want it I think represents a future where we can make it easier and easier for patients to follow care plans, to play active, captain-like roles in their own health in partnership with their care teams and their physicians. And I think if we can remove a lot of the friction that historically exists between those processes, whether it's just information not following or there's a lot to keep track of or a lot of old information to parse and bring in, we can do a tremendous amount of good or we can help patients themselves be empowered to do a tremendous amount of good in their own care plans. And you know as a physician that it's hard to give as much time as you would like because you're always going to have more patients who you have to deal with then you have hours in the day. And it's interesting to see kind of a technology that has infinite time, infinite patients to be able to do that as a compliment to that. If there's one thing that healthcare professionals are short on, it's time. And so when we think about our role internally at OpenAI, we often break down the work that we're doing into three buckets: Raise the floor, so make sure that AI and the benefits of AI are accessible to everyone, and that could be patients, that could be healthcare professionals and others working in health-related industries. Sweep the floor, which means help doctors and help other health professionals save time from the tremendous administration and bureaucratic burdens that they have every day so that they can spend more time with their patients. And then thirdly, raise the ceiling. The impact that AI can have in healthcare I think will allow us to look back on this space in a few years and say, "Wow, we have all accelerated together in a way that medicine is still in the driver's seat, but is also far more empowered than ever before." I don't think anybody feels like their doctor spent too much time with them. So it looks like this is going to be helpful to solve for that. What has been your favorite "aha" or "wow" or "this is a really cool" moment in the intersection of AI and healthcare? I'll answer your question in a non-standard way, which is I think the most amazing thing to see for me in the last year has been the rate of adoption of health actually even beyond the ChatGPT Health product before we announced the ChatGPT Health product. It's been one of our fastest-growing use cases—kind of health and wellness questions—and we shared that hundreds of millions of people a week are starting to use ChatGPT for health and wellness. I think seeing that rapid growth, especially coming from a background of being motivated to work on this problem because I felt like healthcare and clinical AI world were not super aware of the potential of LLMs in healthcare and seeing how far we had come, I think has been a really special moment for me. There's no doubt that the adoption of this technology and the fact that it is increasingly collaborative with the healthcare system and it is increasingly driving feedback loops back to us to improve the models is the most meaningful thing and the most mission-aligned thing. But what I also get excited about is what our research team is increasingly being able to give back to them using that feedback. Not only is it the capabilities of the models, but it's what can be unlocked once those models are allowed to run longer and have more context. And we're starting to see discoveries of medications that have been sitting on a shelf that all of a sudden AI has found ways for them to have meaningful and direct value in patient lives. It is starting to scale experiments that we as individuals wouldn't have been able to juggle on our own. Now that you've been working on this for some time, you've been engaging with clinicians and talking to people helping to deploy this, what has been some of the feedback you've seen? I think the experience of flying to Nairobi and seeing the clinicians using the tool and the ways in which we did this thing which we call active change management where we work really closely with these clinicians and flew to Kenya a couple times to think about the ways that we could deepen their workflows using the AI tool and make it something that not only made sense to them, but actually became something that was indispensable for them. And so as we were concluding the study, the team at Panda Health was actually thinking about potentially running another study. And they actually had a lot of hesitance around running another study because that would have involved having some group of clinicians using AI and some group of clinicians not using AI. It's the patient who had been bouncing around the system for years, the unsolved diagnosis, the emergency where information wasn't present and suddenly being able to step in and assist and accelerate and bring people into the care that could really help is truly a privilege. It's exciting. It's an amplifier and every doctor I know wants to be able to do more for their patients. Thank you very much, this has been very interesting guys. Thank you.

Peter Beck — We're scaling Electron faster than SpaceX scaled Falcon 9

Relentless(16.3K) ·65:19 ·936 views · 2026-03-16
Peter Beck (Founder and CEO, Rocket Lab)
Rocket Lab’s success is built on extreme capital efficiency and a "relentless" engineering culture that prioritizes beauty and producibility, enabling the company to scale launch cadences faster than the Falcon 9 with significantly fewer resources.
TL;DR Rocket Lab’s success is built on extreme capital efficiency and a "relentless" engineering culture that prioritizes beauty and producibility, enabling the company to scale launch cadences faster than the Falcon 9 with significantly fewer resources. Stewardship of Capital:...

TL;DR

Rocket Lab’s success is built on extreme capital efficiency and a "relentless" engineering culture that prioritizes beauty and producibility, enabling the company to scale launch cadences faster than the Falcon 9 with significantly fewer resources.

Capital Efficiency and the "Rutherford" Philosophy

  • Stewardship of Capital: Peter Beck emphasizes that Rocket Lab was built on a fraction of the budget of its competitors. While Elon Musk started SpaceX with $100 million, Beck started with effectively nothing, necessitating a culture of intense resourcefulness.
      • "We have no money, so we have to think": This quote from Ernest Rutherford defines the company’s approach to engineering.
      • Resourcefulness in early days: Beck describes scavenging junk yards for Swagelok fittings because the company could only afford the $3.20 ferrules, not the $50 complete fittings.
  • Vertical Integration as Necessity: Beck notes that vertical integration isn't a "religion" for the company but a necessity to avoid unreliable suppliers who frequently "shit the bed" on timelines and quality.
  • Public Market Accountability: Unlike private companies that may become "fat and happy," being a public company provides a forcing function for rigor, profitability, and long-term sustainability.

The "Rocket Lab Hustle" and Engineering Culture

  • Relentless Execution: The culture is defined by "dogged determination." Beck notes that employees who want a standard eight-to-five job do not survive at Rocket Lab.
  • Overcoming Barriers: Beck highlights the company's ability to "overwhelm the barrier" when suppliers fail.
      • Example: When a supplier failed to deliver titanium pressure vessels, Rocket Lab developed their own 3D printing and welding techniques over a single weekend to stay on schedule.
  • Beauty as a Quality Metric: Inherited from his family's high standards in their home garage, Beck believes that building "beautiful" hardware is a proxy for pride and attention to detail.
      • Philosophy: "It takes no longer to build something beautiful than it does to build a piece of crap." Beautifully executed products are more likely to work because they imply deep thought at every level.
  • No-Blame Environment: When a rocket engine exploded during a critical test before a visit from propulsion experts, the team didn't panic or assign blame. They worked through the weekend to rebuild the engine and test cell, ultimately improving performance by 13%.

Scaling Launch and Production

  • Scaling Cadence: Electron scaled from its first flight to its 50th flight faster than SpaceX’s Falcon 9.
  • Designed for Production: Beck attributes this speed to designing the rocket for manufacture from day one, rather than just designing for flight.
      • Production Hell: Transitioning from R&D to volume production is described as a "whole different set of pain," but the Electron team is now described as "animals" who "bang those vehicles out" with little CEO intervention.
  • The "Rocket Lab Operating System": The company uses a standardized approach to take hideously complex components and make them highly producible and reliable.

Technical Evolution: Electron to Neutron

  • Material Science: While SpaceX moved to stainless steel for Starship, Rocket Lab remains committed to carbon composites for Neutron.
      • Mass Advantage: Carbon composites are four times lighter than stainless steel for the same strength, which allows the rocket engines to be "unstressed" rather than pushed to their absolute limits.
  • Automated Fiber Placement (AFP): Rocket Lab uses AFP machines to scale the manufacturing of carbon structures quickly and cheaply.
  • Neutron Improvements: Neutron is designed to be less mass-sensitive than Electron. It features modular fluid and electrical systems that can be built offline and "plugged in," avoiding the manual labor-intensive piping and cabling required for Electron.

Future Strategic Outlook

  • Diversified Revenue: Launch only represents one-third of the company; the other two-thirds is focused on Space Systems (satellite components and spacecraft).
  • Interplanetary Ambitions: Rocket Lab has already sent a spacecraft to the moon (Capstone) and has two on the way to Mars (Escapade). Beck views these "impossible" missions as essential for building credibility with customers like NASA.
  • End-to-End Infrastructure: Beck believes the major space companies of the future will be "blurry" entities—combinations of AI, communications, and infrastructure. Rocket Lab's goal is to own the "ride" (launch) and the "infrastructure" (satellites) to provide Earth-based services.

"The rocket gods are trying to crush you every chance they get. So you have to be a little bit sunny side up." — Peter Beck

The moment that a few of those things aren't true—like, you can make some money at it and it doesn't require a nation of energy to achieve it—then that naturally democratizes into commercial entities. And, you know, that's exactly what's happened with space. Like, it no longer takes a nation to build a satellite. You know, a few college kids in a dorm can go and build a little satellite. Mom and dad at home are just super stoked to see someone win. I think it is, like I say, it feels like to me largely a construct of the media. What about your culture enables you to be able to tackle these massively difficult problems and do it in an efficient way? That's a great question. Like, yeah, if you've got kids, you've got a number one and then your number twos and then there's like the number threes. And that was the supplier. The supplier's like number threes everywhere. So, that was just a total disaster. And I think most companies would have stopped, whereas we just developed a technique to 3D print this titanium pressure vessel and developed a whole bunch of welding processes ourselves. And we produced the tanks in time to meet the date for the launch vehicle and qualified them and acceptance tested them and everything. So now there's three suppliers in the United States who can build thin-wall titanium tanks. And what's more is because we 3D printed them rather than traditional forming them, we can make whatever shape or anything anybody wants anytime. So, I mean, it's those kind of things where I think, historically, you hit a barrier and people kind of go, "I don't know what we're going to do, we're going to stop," whereas we'll just overwhelm the barrier or we'll dig under it or we'll fly over it. Like, it's just not acceptable to stop. Is there almost like a culture of just "fuck it, let's do it"? Yeah, a hundred percent. Yeah. And like scary things, you know, they're scary but we'll still go after them anyway. And when you're kind of running into one of those problems where hypothetically if there was a bunch of suppliers that could just fulfill and reduce all your cognitive load and difficulty of making the thing, that would be great, but obviously there really isn't? No, that'd be great. Oh man, I'd wake up in the morning and that would be a dream. It's like suppliers that deliver on time, to budget, at a reasonable price—that would be amazing. But unfortunately, that is very rare. What's the worst supplier experience you've ever had? I mean, it's just we have people... there's a whole team that just sits in suppliers' buildings just trying to juggle things along. You've been at this for now like 26 years. What's the best example of Rocket Lab hustle? Well, I gave you that tank one there a moment ago, but I mean, it's defined by a set of behaviors. You can come to this facility and you can drive past at any time of the night or the morning and there's lights on. Is there any really good story that you have of having to basically sleep here and un-fuck some bottleneck that you had over the past couple years? Oh my God, there's so many. I mean, like in the customer room there's beds and pillows for anybody to use. So I mean that happens all the time. One thing I can think of as many years ago is that we had developed this rocket engine, and it was a very unique rocket engine. We had a bunch of really important people coming over from the propulsion industry to look at this engine. And we were doing the last test on a Friday afternoon. And we thought, "Oh, we'll just do this one last test before we put the engine to bed because these guys will turn up on Monday." So we did this test and a hydraulic line was fatiguing. We didn't realize and it failed. The hydraulic line failure caused basically the valving sequence to be wrong and the whole engine exploded. And not only did it... it was so good, it was such a good explosion. It's only a sort of a 500-pound engine, so it's not a big engine, but it exploded so good that it just tore everything out of the test cell as well. There was... we just stood and walked back in there after it all sorted out and there was just shit hanging off the walls and it was just a total mess. And meanwhile these guys had just taken off from the States and they were on their way, going to land. They're going to land whether or not the engine is there? There's nothing left. Not even a test cell. So, we're all standing around and like there wasn't one person that said, "Oh no," or not one person said, "Well, that's us screwed." People just said, "Okay, well, there's some titanium back in the shop, I'll get back there now and I'll start machining." The electrician's like, "Yep, okay, I've got some Cat 5 cable, start rewiring this and we'll do this." And everybody just got to work. And the next day, I got a call from the guys that were coming to visit us and they said, "Well, actually we landed here on Saturday morning, so we're all feeling good, so why don't we just come down to the test cell and we'll start work right now?" And we're like, "Oh no, you'll be jet-lagged, you don't want to do that, wait until Monday. Just wait until Monday because you'll feel much better, trust me, this jet lag to New Zealand really hits you." Don't want you to be sleep deprived when you come. Oh yeah, exactly right. So we just, you know, nobody slept at all. And we just worked and worked and worked. And we were rebuilding this engine and we knew there was a couple of things in the engine that were like, you know, we would have liked to fix at some point in time, but they were risky. Like, if we made some changes to an injector, it might not have worked. But we're like, "Oh well, at this point, let's just go for it." So we made a whole bunch of changes to the engine, and we changed what we thought some of the injectors and some of the parts of the engine we thought would improve its performance. And then we got this whole engine back together and got it on the test cell on Monday morning. And our very important friends turned up. And they're like, "Hey guys, how's it going?" We go, "Good, good, right, ready for an engine test? Yep." And we hot-fired that engine, and that was the first time that engine had been hot-fired since a rebuilt engine, a rebuilt test cell, and a brand new engine with all these modifications. And I think most companies would have played it safe and just said "Let's rebuild it exactly as it was," but we built this new engine and that new engine performed 13 percent better than the previous one, which in rocket engine perspective is a huge number. Over the course of a weekend? Yeah, and completely rebuilt the engine. And that engine ran for the next nearly a month, just day after day doing hot fire after hot fire and tweaking things and doing different experiments that everybody wanted to do and all the rest of it. So, that's a Rocket Lab hustle. I mean it's just, you know, I think a lot of people would go, "Well, yeah, pretty much." What happens after that sort of event like internally? How do people kind of react to being able to overcome something that feels insurmountable or impossible? That's a great question. Generally, there's the next big thing, so you're just onto the next big thing. And there's been a couple of these kind of really big things, like the Capstone mission. The Capstone mission was a really big one; that was a totally impossible project and I still to this day don't know how we pulled it off, but that was a totally impossible project. Do you want to just walk through what that was? Why it was so impossible? That was our mission to the moon. So, we had signed the Capstone mission and we had to deliver the spacecraft to the moon and it's off an Electron rocket. So, going to the moon off a tiny little rocket is like seven to nine million bucks? Yeah, it was ten million dollars. But it... like on paper it closed, but in practice, it was the most ridiculously hard thing. We were measuring grams in that rocket. And there was like a three-hour debate whether or not we could afford the mass of the NASA stickers down the side of the rocket because the mass of those stickers would, you know, put us over the edge. So, it was the most ridiculously difficult engineering project. And we delivered that spacecraft to the moon with such perfect accuracy, it was incredible. But there were so many firsts there. The rocket engine that was on the little spacecraft that took it to the moon was the highest performing engine that we have ever built. And we had a separate tank of 11 herbs and spices that we would inject into the engine to just make it run super hot. So, it was the craziest project. Even today you can ask people around the company what their best moment was at Rocket Lab, and anybody working on Capstone or just about anybody will say Capstone, even though it was the most punishing and cruelest mission that we ever did. What's your kind of philosophy on chewing glass and enjoying taking on extremely difficult challenges with uncertainty on whether or not they can even be successful? Yeah, well, I think that is the definition of a good startup. Yeah, I mean I think that's probably similar. Like, I'm not adding value to anything that's running great. It might feel good, but I'm not adding any value to that whatsoever. You can analyze and analyze to a certain point, but at some point you actually just have to press the button.
