Things that are going wrong and things that require big decisions with no information, and then of course just pushing the company in the right direction. In the past, people have kind of joked about Elon time being maybe even years late on some projects. And over time he's kind of course-corrected to trying to get down to roughly 50 percent of the time whatever the timeline is that he gives is going to be right. That means by definition half the time it's going to be off, but because he's pulling in timelines, more things happen. If you're doing this mission where you're sending something to Mars or sending something to the moon, how do you decide how to structure that timeline so that you know that you're actually going to be able to ship? So, I guess you have to be 50 percent entrepreneurial and 50 percent engineer. As a public company, you can't just stand up there and wave your arms around, because some people do do that. Yeah, but you get in trouble for that. It doesn't work. Yeah, no, that's... I mean, you have to be respectful to your shareholders and your investors that they're making decisions off your timelines. Sometimes you get it wrong, and that's unfortunate, but generally, the dates and things that we try and put out there, we try and be right. Now, we always run "green light schedules," meaning that when we put a schedule together there's not some big fat in there that says if this doesn't work, we built a bit extra time in here and built a bit extra time in here. Because engineers will take as long as you have allocated them to do a job. If you allocate all the fat in there, then that is your default best schedule. Whereas we've always run green light schedules from day one, where you put the schedule down in place that you think is if everything goes right. And if everything goes right, then that is the best time that you're going to achieve. Of course, not everything always goes right. But you can guarantee where you build fat in, it'll be the wrong place anyway. So, that has been very effective for Rocket Lab, and yep, okay, sometimes we're late on some stuff. But it's not horrifically late, and it's still faster than if you had done a more traditional approach. If you do have a situation where things do go late, what do you do to basically make sure that whatever the new timeline is happens as quickly as possible? Well, I mean, there's nobody at Rocket Lab that doesn't feel the anxiety if something is not going well. Everybody here is a super high performer and they want... it's very important for their own ego to deliver beautiful stuff on time. So, it's self-policing in that respect. And look, the stuff we do is freaking hard. Some of it has never been done before. So inevitably, you have issues. and I'd say one of the great things about the company is like it's not a culture where if somebody has a bad day, everybody runs away and points at them and going, "Oh no, glad that's not me." It's the opposite, right? Everybody runs towards the thing that's on fire to try and put it out and offers their help and assistance. So, as far as a culture goes, in some cultures everybody is standing on each other's throat to try and get ahead. Not here. Everybody runs to the thing that's on fire to try and put it out. How do you actually like foster that type of culture? Well, I mean, I wish I could say that there's like a statement or anything. It's just the way the company is; it's just the way the company always has been. You know, right back to that example I gave you of that engine blowing up, nobody's jumping around and screaming, everybody just gets to work. I remember you said we make the "Ferrari of rockets" and you really care about design and beauty and function. What's your philosophy around building beautiful amazing products that work and why does it matter to create things that are great? Well, it comes down to my belief that if you build something beautifully, then you've taken pride in what you've done. If you've taken pride in what you've done, then chances are you've looked a little bit deeper than your own work. And if everybody is building beautiful things, if somebody doesn't build a beautiful thing, then it becomes really, really obvious. Generally, if you build beautiful things, they work. It's very rare that you build something that is just beautifully executed and beautifully thought about that's just a piece of crap. It doesn't happen that often. Whereas I'd say if you build a piece of crap, it's a miracle or it's a win if it works. So, and I honestly believe it takes no longer to build something beautiful than it does to throw something together that's a piece of crap. By the time you spend all the time going back and rebuilding bits because clearances weren't right or code wasn't right or whatever, I don't think it takes any longer. But we used to have a saying on the wall: "Make everything you do a work of art." Because if it doesn't work and looks like crap, you've got nothing, but if it doesn't work and looks beautiful, at least it looks beautiful. So, this is an ingrained culture. And it doesn't just apply to hardware; it's like code, it's software, it's the facilities you're in. We're here to build beautiful things; we're not here to make crap. Who are the people or things that have inspired you on that kind of philosophy and trying to create beautiful products? I don't know. It's probably just my upbringing. As a child, that's what we were always expected to do. We were very practical kids. We were building go-karts and building these kinds of things. Whether it be my father or my brother or anything, like you would know if you built something that was rough. Like the whole family would go, "That's a piece of shit, why'd you build it like that?" So there was an expectation and a pride that everything that came out of the Beck garage was beautiful. How do you marry those two philosophies of going to junk yards to find some little piece you can salvage for a few hundred bucks while also understanding that the finished version is going to be like this gorgeous thing? Well, I mean, if you clean a fitting, it looks exactly like a brand new fitting, so you're not compromising anything in that respect. And you're putting a new ferrule on it, so that's the bit that's doing the work. So I think they're not separate things. A piece of art that's done by an artist that sells for $500 versus something that sells for five million—I mean, they're still beautiful. So you don't need to equate a price tag to beauty and quality of engineering. I remember you mentioned that the most stressful part of your life is launch day. I hate launch day. Electron is a great product, but if we look at how we've gone about Neutron, it's just so much better from a production standpoint. What about it is better? Well, it's just, firstly, it's less mass sensitive than Electron. Like, Electron, every gram counts. So you have to do a whole bunch of things from a production standpoint that are suboptimal but from a mass perspective are critical. Whereas on Neutron, we don't have that same constraint. And it's just really nice stuff like all of the fluids systems are just built completely off the rocket and then just assembled in as boom-boom-boom systems. Whereas on Electron, you're literally having to bend tubes to pipe stuff up and pipe stuff in. And then same with the electrical systems. The complete electrical system is built off the rocket and then just one day it gets installed in the entire rocket. Whereas you're not pulling cables up and down the rocket like you are in an Electron. So there's just a whole bunch of stuff that we could do with Neutron that's just way better.
